Conditions of Wakalah: Principal, Agent, and Subject Matter
Conditions for validity of agency regarding all parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of conditions for a valid agency contract in Hanafi fiqh and identify which conditions affect validity.

Core Idea
Agency is valid if the principal has capacity, the agent is sane, and the subject matter is known and not a hadd punishment (except theft/slander).

In Hanafi law, the validity of an agency contract (wakalah) depends on conditions relating to the principal (muwakkil), the agent (wakil), and the subject matter (muwakkal bih). This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq categorizes these conditions, highlighting key requirements and internal Hanafi disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Recognize that conditions of wakalah are divided into those pertaining to the principal, the agent, and the subject matter.
This tripartite division is standard in Hanafi works.
2
Examine principal's conditions
Determine that the principal must have the capacity to perform the act himself; this is a condition for validity.
The passage defers detailed discussion to a later section.
3
Examine agent's conditions
Identify that the agent must be sane (aql), but not necessarily free, pubescent, or non-apostate. Note the internal dispute on whether the agent's knowledge of the agency is a condition.
The text presents two views from al-Ziyadat and al-Wakala.
4
Examine subject matter's conditions
Recognize that agency cannot involve establishing or executing a hadd punishment except for theft and slander. Also, there must be no gross ignorance (jihala mufahhasha) regarding the subject matter.
Abu Yusuf expands the exception to all hudud and qisas.

The conditions are of types: what relates to the principal, what relates to the agent, and what relates to the subject matter. What relates to the principal is that he be someone who has the capacity to do what he authorizes himself; we will discuss it when commenting on the book. What relates to the agent is reason; so the agency of a mad person and an unintelligent child is invalid; not puberty, freedom, and not apostasy, so the agency of an apostate is valid and does not require suspension because the suspended is his ownership. Knowledge of the agent of the agency: if he authorized him and he did not know, then he acts, it is suspended pending the principal's ratification or the agent's later. Al-Badai' related a disagreement: in al-Ziyadat it is a condition, and in al-Wakala it is not a condition. Knowledge is established either by direct conversation, or by writing to him, or by a messenger to him, or by the report of two laymen, or one trustworthy or untrustworthy if the agent believes him; otherwise, according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is not established, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is. As for what relates to the subject matter, it should not be establishing or executing a hadd punishment except the hadd of theft and slander. Abu Yusuf generalized hadd and qisas on the disagreement. And that there be no gross ignorance (jihala mufahhasha) as will come. The command? His statement: (And he stated in al-Nihaya...) I say: What preceded in the chapter of khiyar al-ru'ya, quoting from al-Fawa'id, made it from the wordings of risala not from wordings of tawkeel, and it will come in the chapter of agency for litigation that it is not tawkeel. His statement: (Know that not every command constitutes tawkeel...) the gist is that it is necessary that the command indicates that the commanded person does the act on behalf of the commander by way of deputyship. His statement: (And in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi...) the gist is what the author mentioned in khiyar al-ru'ya where he said: In al-Mi'raj it is said that the difference between the messenger and the agent is that the agent does not attribute the contract to the principal, while the messenger cannot dispense with attributing it to the sender. This is indicated by the verse 'O Messenger, convey' [5:67] and His saying 'And you are not a guardian over them' [6:107] which negates agency and affirms message. His statement: (No) for puberty and freedom. al-Ramli said: So the agency of a minor who has reason and a slave is valid for marriage, divorce, khul', reconciliation, borrowing, gift, sale, purchase, rent, and everything that the principal can do himself. Understand. His statement: (As for what relates to the subject matter) al-Ramli said: And from it is general agency. And he authored...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions (shurut) for a valid agency contract in Hanafi law, categorized by the three parties: principal (muwakkil), agent (wakil), and subject matter (muwakkal bih). For the principal, he must have legal capacity to perform the act himself. For the agent, the key condition is sanity (aql); a mad person or an unintelligent child cannot be agents. However, puberty, freedom, and non-apostasy are not conditions: a discerning child or a slave can be agents, and an apostate's agency is valid (though his ownership may be suspended). Knowledge of the agency by the agent is a point of disagreement: according to al-Ziyadat it is a condition, according to al-Wakala it is not. If the agent is unaware but still acts, it is suspended pending ratification. Knowledge can be established through various means. For the subject matter, agency cannot be for establishing or executing a hadd punishment except for theft and slander. Abu Yusuf generalized to all hudud and qisas. Additionally, there must be no gross ignorance (jihala mufahhasha) in the subject matter. The passage then repeats earlier points about the distinction between tawkeel and irsal, emphasizing that the phrase must indicate deputyship. Finally, al-Ramli's comment confirms that a minor with reason and a slave can be agents for various contracts.

Key Terms
شرائط (shara'it): Conditions that must be fulfilled for the validity of the agency contract.
جهالة متفاحشة (jihala mufahhasha): Gross ignorance about the subject matter that renders the contract invalid.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law for certain crimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principal must have capacity to perform the act himself.
B) The agent must be sane.
C) The agent must be a free Muslim adult.
D) The subject matter must not involve gross ignorance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi wakalah, the key conditions are the principal's capacity, the agent's sanity, and the subject matter being lawful and known; other conditions like freedom and puberty are not required.

Conditions of Agency (Wakala) and the Distinction Between Wakil and Rasul
Expanding on the conditions of agency with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of conditions for a valid agency contract and distinguish between a wakil and a rasul according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Agency requires the agent to act as a principal's deputy, not merely a messenger — and the agent must know of his appointment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the essential conditions for a valid agency (wakala) in Hanafi fiqh. It distinguishes between an agent (wakil) who directly performs contracts on behalf of the principal and a messenger (rasul) who merely conveys the contract. The key condition for the agent is sanity and, according to the stronger Hanafi position, knowledge of the agency. Understanding these conditions prevents common mistakes in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between wakil and rasul
Identify the functional difference: a wakil directly performs the contract and does not attribute it to the principal; a rasul merely conveys the contract and attributes it to the principal. Note the Quranic evidence cited.
The passage also clarifies that not every command constitutes tawkeel; there must be an indication of deputyship.
2
Identify the three categories of conditions
Conditions relate to the principal, the agent, and the subject matter. List the conditions for each category as discussed in the passage.
Principal: must be capable of the act himself. Agent: must be sane and discerning. Subject matter: must not involve establishment/execution of hadd except theft/slander, and must not have excessive uncertainty.
3
Focus on the agent's knowledge condition
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa requires the agent to know of the agency; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) do not require it. The practical consequence is that if the agent acts without knowledge, under Abu Hanifa's view the action is suspended until the principal permits.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the position of al-Ziyadat (Zahir al-Riwayah).
4
Apply the knowledge condition in practice
How is knowledge established? Direct address, letter, messenger, or report. There is a sub-dispute on whether the reporter must be reliable. According to Abu Hanifa, if the agent believes an unreliable reporter, it suffices; otherwise not. The Sahibayn accept it more broadly.
This affects the validity of actions taken by an agent who learned through indirect or questionable means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة 237 العبد. وقد يكون مضافاً إلى وقت بأن يوكله في بيع هذا العبد غداً ويصير وكيلاً في الغد وما بعده لا قبله ا هـ . .. فإن قلت : فما الفرق بين التوكيل والإرسال فإن الإذن والأمر توكيل كما علمت؟ قلت : الرسول أن يقول له أرسلتك أو كن رسولاً عني في كذا وقد جعل منها الزيلعي في باب خيار الرؤية أمرتك بقبضه . وصرح في النهاية فيه معزياً إلى الفوائد الظهيرية أنه من التوكيل وهو الموافق لما في The slave. And it may be attached to a time, such as appointing him to sell this slave tomorrow, so he becomes an agent tomorrow and after, not before. End. If you ask: what is the difference between tawkeel and irsal, for idhn and amr are tawkeel as you know? I say: the rasul is to say 'I sent you' or 'Be a messenger from me in such-and-such', and al-Zayla'i in the chapter of khiyar al-ru'ya included 'I commanded you to take possession' among them. And it is explicitly stated in al-Nihayah, citing al-Fawaid al-Zahiriyyah, that it is from tawkeel, and that agrees with what is in al-Bada'i', for there is no difference between 'do such-and-such' and 'I command you to do such-and-such'. Know that not every command benefits tawkeel; in al-Walwaljiyyah: he gave him a thousand and said 'buy for me with it' or 'sell', or said 'buy with it' or 'sell' and did not say 'for me' — that is not tawkeel. Likewise 'buy with this thousand a slave-girl' and he pointed to his own property; if he said 'buy a slave-girl for a thousand dirhams' it is advice, and what the commanded person buys is for him, not the commander. Likewise if he said 'buy this for a thousand' unless he added 'I will give you a dirham for your purchase' because stipulating a fee indicates deputyship. End. In Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: the wakil is the one who directly performs the contract, and the rasul is the one who conveys the direct performance, and the commodity is a trust in their hands. End. I only said 'in acceptance even if virtually' to include the fourth silence. Its conditions are types: what relates to the principal, what relates to the agent, and what relates to the subject matter. What relates to the principal is that he be one who can perform the act himself, and we will discuss it when explaining the book. What relates to the agent is sanity: so it is not valid to appoint an insane person or a child who does not understand, but not puberty, freedom, or lack of apostasy, so it is valid to appoint an apostate and it is not suspended because what is suspended is his ownership. And the agent's knowledge of the agency: if he appointed him and he did not know, then his action is suspended on the principal's or agent's subsequent permission. It is reported in al-Bada'i' that there is disagreement: in al-Ziyadat it is a condition, in al-Wakala it is not a condition. Knowledge is established either by direct address, or a letter to him, or a messenger to him, or by the report of two laymen, or one reliable person, or an unreliable person if the agent believes him; otherwise, according to him (Abu Hanifa) no, according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) yes. As for what relates to the subject matter: that it not involve establishment or execution of a hadd except the hadd of theft and slander. Abu Yusuf generalized hadd and qisas with disagreement, and that there be no excessive ignorance (jahalah mufahisha) as will come. Al-Amr: His saying: 'And it is explicitly stated in al-Nihayah...' I say: what preceded in the chapter of khiyar al-ru'ya quoting from al-Fawaid considered it from the phrases of risala, not from the phrases of tawkeel, and it will come in the chapter of agency for litigation that it is not tawkeel. His saying: 'Know that not every command benefits tawkeel...' its summary is that there must be in the command something indicating that the commanded person does the commander's act by way of deputyship. His saying: 'In Tahdhib al-Qalanisi...' its summary is what the author mentioned in the chapter of khiyar al-ru'ya where he said: in al-Mi'raj it is said the difference between the rasul and the wakil is that the wakil does not attribute the contract to the principal, and the rasul cannot dispense with attributing it to the sender, and the indication is in His saying 'O messenger, convey' (5:67) and His saying 'And you are not over them a wakil' (6:107) which negates agency and affirms messengership. End. His saying: '(not) puberty and freedom' — al-Ramli said: i.e., it is valid to appoint a discerning child and a slave in marriage, divorce, khul', sulh, borrowing, gift, sale, purchase, lease, and everything the principal can contract himself, so understand. His saying: 'As for what relates to the subject matter' — al-Ramli said: from it is general agency. And he authored...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the conditions of agency (wakala). It opens by clarifying the difference between tawkeel (appointing an agent) and irsal (sending a messenger). The key point is that mere command (amr) is not always tawkeel; there must be an indication that the agent is acting on behalf of the principal (niyaba). The passage then categorizes the conditions into three types: those relating to the principal, the agent, and the subject matter. For the principal, the condition is that he must be capable of performing the act himself. For the agent, sanity is a condition; a child who does not understand cannot be agent, but a discerning child or a slave can be appointed for certain contracts. There is a notable intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the agent's knowledge of the agency: according to al-Ziyadat it is a condition, while in al-Wakala it is not. Knowledge can be established through various means, and there is a difference between Abu Hanifa (who requires the agent to believe the informant) and the two companions (who accept even an unreliable report if the agent believes it). For the subject matter, it cannot involve establishing or executing a hadd penalty except for theft and slander. The passage concludes with a commentary (al-Amr) that further refines the definitions of wakil and rasul, citing Quranic verses.

Key Terms
التوكيل (al-tawkeel): The act of appointing someone as an agent (wakil) to perform a legal act on one's behalf.
الرسول (al-rasul): A messenger who conveys words or commands but does not directly perform the contract; the contract is attributed to the principal.
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent who directly performs a contract on behalf of the principal, and the contract is attributed to him, not the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), what is the ruling if a principal appoints an agent but the agent does not know of the appointment, and then the agent acts?
A) The action is valid immediately.
B) The action is void.
C) The action is suspended on subsequent permission.
D) The action is valid if the agent is adult.

Key Takeaway
For a valid agency, the agent must be sane and, according to the stronger Hanafi position, must know of his appointment; otherwise, his actions are suspended until the principal permits.

Dispute Resolution in Agency: Whose Word is Accepted?
Ruling on dispute resolution in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the agent's word is accepted regarding discharge from liability to the principal, while the creditor's word is accepted regarding non-receipt, and determine the consequences for oaths and recourse.

Core Idea
The agent is believed for his discharge; the creditor is believed for non-receipt.

When an agent is appointed to pay a debt, disputes may arise: the agent claims he paid, the creditor denies, and the principal may be involved. The Hanafi school lays out a nuanced rule that balances trust in the agent with protection of the creditor's rights. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides the framework for resolving such disputes, including the role of oaths and the possibility of the agent seeking recourse from the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
In the dispute, there are three parties: principal (creditor or owner), agent (mukallaf), and the recipient (creditor or third party). The agent claims payment; the recipient denies receipt.
This sets up the classic scenario: agent says 'I paid', creditor says 'I didn't receive'.
2
Apply the rule: whose word is accepted
The rule states: 'al-qawl li al-wakil fi barā'atihi wa li al-dā'in fi 'adam qabḍihi'. The agent's word is accepted regarding his own discharge (i.e., he is free of liability to the principal). The creditor's word is accepted regarding non-receipt, so the debt remains owed by the principal.
This means the agent is not liable to the principal, but the principal still owes the creditor.
3
Determine the oath requirement
An oath is required from one of them. The passage says: 'yahlifu man kadhdhabahu al-muwakkil dūna man ṣaddaqahu'. The agent takes an oath if the principal denied him (i.e., the principal didn't confirm payment); if the principal confirmed, no oath on the agent.
The oath is on the agent if the principal denies, or on the creditor if the principal denies? Actually, 'man kadhdhabahu al-muwakkil' refers to the agent being denied by the principal.
4
Analyze the recourse between agent and principal
If the principal confirms the agent's payment, the agent can seek recourse from the principal for the amount paid (per al-Quduri). However, al-Jami' says no recourse; al-Bada'i' prefers the first view, making it the mufta bihi.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the preferred view allows the agent to recover from the principal.
5
Apply to analogous cases
Similar rules apply to deposits and other trust properties: if the depositor orders payment and the recipient denies, the recipient's word is taken with an oath, and the agent must prove payment.
The principle extends to all cases where an agent is ordered to deliver property.

Source Text

The fifth: on its rulings. From them: the establishment of the authority to dispose of what the agency covers. From them: that he may not delegate except with permission or general authorization. From them: that he is a trustee (amin) regarding what is in his hand like the depositary; he is liable for what the depositary is liable for, and he is discharged by what the depositary is discharged by, and his word is accepted regarding discharge from liability. So if someone gave him money and said 'pay this to so-and-so from my debt' and he said 'I paid it' and the creditor denied it, then the word is the agent's regarding his discharge, and the creditor's word regarding non-receipt, so his debt is not canceled. Oath is required from one of them: the one whom the principal denied takes the oath, not the one whom he confirmed. By analogy, if the depositor ordered him to pay it to so-and-so and he claimed it, and so-and-so denied it; even if the property was a liability on a man, like usurped property in the hand of the usurper, or a debt owed to the claimant, and the claimant or the owner of the usurped property ordered the man to pay it to so-and-so, and the commanded person said 'I paid it to him' and so-and-so said 'I did not receive it', then the word is so-and-so's that he did not receive it, and the agent is not believed regarding the payment except with evidence or the principal's confirmation. Neither is believed regarding the recipient; the word is his with oath. The agent may ask the principal to take an oath that he does not know whether it was paid; if he declines, liability is removed from him. If he did not pay anything, but merely ordered him to pay his debt from his own wealth, and he claimed it, and the claimant and the principal denied it, and there is no evidence, then the word is theirs with oath, and the principal takes an oath of ignorance. If the principal confirmed him but the claimant did not, then he (the agent) may seek recourse from him (the principal) for what he claimed, and the claimant also seeks his debt from the principal; al-Quduri mentioned this. In al-Jami': there is no recourse for the agent against his principal even if he confirmed him; but the first is more appropriate as in al-Bada'i'. If the depositor claimed that he ordered him to pay it except to so-and-so, and the owner denied it, then the word is his (the depositor) that he did not order. A fatwa incident occurred at the time of writing this: someone gave money to another to give to a third, then they differed on its designation; the commander said 'I ordered you to give it to Zayd', and the commanded said 'to Amr, and I gave it to him'. The answer was that the word is the agent's because they agreed on the original permission, so he is a trustee. For this reason al-Zayla'i said at the end of mudaraba: if someone gives money and they differ, the giver saying mudaraba and the recipient saying deposit, the word is the recipient's because they agreed on permission. End. From its rulings: he is not obligated to do what he was appointed for except in returning a deposit: if he said 'give this cloth to so-and-so' and he accepts, and the commander disappears, the commanded person is compelled to give it. As for other things, he is not obligated to execute; thus in al-Muhit, and its completion in our benefits. From them: in al-Bazzaziyyah: if he appoints him to collect his deposit and gives him a fee, it is valid; but if he appoints him to collect his debt and gives him a fee, it is not valid unless he specifies a known period. Likewise the agent for litigation if he specifies a period it is permissible, and the agent for litigation; thus in al-Walwaljiyyah. From its rulings: it is not invalidated by conditions. End. Jabr: the author of this book has a treatise called al-Mas'ala al-Khassa fi al-Wakala al-'Amma, and its summary is that the agent with general agency owns everything except divorce, manumission, gift, and charity according to the mufta bihi; its completion is in it. End. I say: the form of general agency preceded at the beginning of these margins, and will also come at the beginning of the next discourse. His saying: 'A fatwa incident occurred...' al-Ramli said: he will mention a branch of the actual situation after a quire and it will come.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is the fifth section (al-khamis) on the rulings of agency (ahkam al-wakala) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It lists multiple ahkam: 1) The agent has authority to transact within the scope of agency. 2) The agent may not delegate unless permitted. 3) The agent is a trustee (amin) like a depositary, with similar liability and discharge rules. The bulk of the passage deals with disputes over payment: if an agent claims he paid a debt to a creditor, and the creditor denies, the agent is believed regarding his own discharge (he is free from liability to the principal), but the creditor's word is accepted regarding non-receipt, so the principal's debt remains. Oaths are required accordingly. There are variations: if the recipient denies receipt, the agent must prove with evidence or the principal's confirmation. If the principal confirms the agent, the agent can seek recourse from the principal for what he paid. The passage cites al-Quduri and al-Jami' with a preferred view from al-Bada'i'. A contemporary fatwa incident is discussed: when the commander and agent differ on whom the money was to be given, the agent's word is taken because they agreed on the original permission, making the agent a trustee. This is analogous to the mudaraba case. Other rulings: the agent is not compelled to perform the task unless it involves returning a deposit when the owner is absent. Agency with a fee: valid for collecting a deposit, but for collecting a debt it requires a specified time. General agency excludes divorce, manumission, gift, and charity according to the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
الأمين (al-amin): A trustee who is trusted with property; he is not liable for loss unless negligent.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or guarantee; in this context, the agent is liable for the property if he breaches trust or if he fails to prove payment.
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath; in disputes, the party whose word is accepted may be required to swear an oath to confirm their claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between an agent and a creditor over whether a debt was paid, whose word is accepted according to Hanafi law?
A) The creditor's word regarding receipt, and the agent's word regarding his own discharge.
B) The agent's word in all matters.
C) The creditor's word in all matters.
D) The principal's word.

Key Takeaway
In agency disputes over payment, the agent is believed for his own discharge, but the creditor's denial preserves the debt against the principal.

The Agent as Trustee and Scope of Authority
Rulings on agent as trustee, compulsion, fees, and general agency scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the agent's status as a trustee (amin) and state the rules regarding delegation, compulsion to perform tasks, agency with fees, and the scope of general agency.

Core Idea
The agent is an amin with limited liability, except for returning deposits; general agency excludes divorce, manumission, gift, and charity.

Beyond dispute resolution, agency law in the Hanafi school defines the agent's role as a trustee, not a guarantor. This lesson covers the agent's liability, when he can be compelled to act, the rules for agency with fees, and what a general agent can and cannot do. These rules ensure clarity in the agent's duties and protect both parties.

Guided Path
1
Establish the agent's status as amin
The passage explicitly states: 'annahu amin fi ma fi yadihi ka al-mudi''. The agent is a trustee, not a guarantor, so he is only liable for negligence or breach.
This means the agent does not guarantee the property; he is trusted like a depositary.
2
Identify when the agent can be compelled to act
Generally, the agent is not forced to execute the task. Exception: returning a deposit (wadi'a) when the owner disappears and the agent has accepted the task.
This exception ensures the safekeeping of property. For other tasks, the agent may refuse.
3
Analyze agency with fees (ujr)
If the agent is appointed to collect a deposit with a fee, it is valid. For collecting a debt, it is invalid unless a specific time period is stated. Similarly, agency for litigation requires a specified period.
The distinction is due to the nature of the task: collecting a deposit is a single act, but collecting a debt may require ongoing effort; uncertainty in duration invalidates the fee contract.
4
Define the scope of general agency (wakala 'amma)
The mufta bihi is that a general agent can do everything except divorce, manumission, gift, and charity. These require explicit authorization.
This is a key limitation: general agency does not cover personal or charitable acts that affect the principal's rights.
5
Apply the rule that agency is not invalidated by conditions
The passage ends with: 'la tabtul bi al-shurut'. Conditions attached to the agency contract do not invalidate it, unless they contradict the purpose of the agency.
This preserves the validity of the agency while allowing flexibility.

The fifth: on its rulings. From them: the establishment of the authority to dispose of what the agency covers. From them: that he may not delegate except with permission or general authorization. From them: that he is a trustee (amin) regarding what is in his hand like the depositary; he is liable for what the depositary is liable for, and he is discharged by what the depositary is discharged by, and his word is accepted regarding discharge from liability. So if someone gave him money and said 'pay this to so-and-so from my debt' and he said 'I paid it' and the creditor denied it, then the word is the agent's regarding his discharge, and the creditor's word regarding non-receipt, so his debt is not canceled. Oath is required from one of them: the one whom the principal denied takes the oath, not the one whom he confirmed. By analogy, if the depositor ordered him to pay it to so-and-so and he claimed it, and so-and-so denied it; even if the property was a liability on a man, like usurped property in the hand of the usurper, or a debt owed to the claimant, and the claimant or the owner of the usurped property ordered the man to pay it to so-and-so, and the commanded person said 'I paid it to him' and so-and-so said 'I did not receive it', then the word is so-and-so's that he did not receive it, and the agent is not believed regarding the payment except with evidence or the principal's confirmation. Neither is believed regarding the recipient; the word is his with oath. The agent may ask the principal to take an oath that he does not know whether it was paid; if he declines, liability is removed from him. If he did not pay anything, but merely ordered him to pay his debt from his own wealth, and he claimed it, and the claimant and the principal denied it, and there is no evidence, then the word is theirs with oath, and the principal takes an oath of ignorance. If the principal confirmed him but the claimant did not, then he (the agent) may seek recourse from him (the principal) for what he claimed, and the claimant also seeks his debt from the principal; al-Quduri mentioned this. In al-Jami': there is no recourse for the agent against his principal even if he confirmed him; but the first is more appropriate as in al-Bada'i'. If the depositor claimed that he ordered him to pay it except to so-and-so, and the owner denied it, then the word is his (the depositor) that he did not order. A fatwa incident occurred at the time of writing this: someone gave money to another to give to a third, then they differed on its designation; the commander said 'I ordered you to give it to Zayd', and the commanded said 'to Amr, and I gave it to him'. The answer was that the word is the agent's because they agreed on the original permission, so he is a trustee. For this reason al-Zayla'i said at the end of mudaraba: if someone gives money and they differ, the giver saying mudaraba and the recipient saying deposit, the word is the recipient's because they agreed on permission. End. From its rulings: he is not obligated to do what he was appointed for except in returning a deposit: if he said 'give this cloth to so-and-so' and he accepts, and the commander disappears, the commanded person is compelled to give it. As for other things, he is not obligated to execute; thus in al-Muhit, and its completion in our benefits. From them: in al-Bazzaziyyah: if he appoints him to collect his deposit and gives him a fee, it is valid; but if he appoints him to collect his debt and gives him a fee, it is not valid unless he specifies a known period. End. I say: the form of general agency preceded at the beginning of these margins, and will also come at the beginning of the next discourse. His saying: 'A fatwa incident occurred...' al-Ramli said: he will mention a branch of the actual situation after a quire and it will come.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is the fifth section (al-khamis) on the rulings of agency (ahkam al-wakala) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It lists multiple ahkam: 1) The agent has authority to transact within the scope of agency. 2) The agent may not delegate unless permitted. 3) The agent is a trustee (amin) like a depositary, with similar liability and discharge rules. The bulk of the passage deals with disputes over payment: if an agent claims he paid a debt to a creditor, and the creditor denies, the agent is believed regarding his own discharge (he is free from liability to the principal), but the creditor's word is accepted regarding non-receipt, so the principal's debt remains. Oaths are required accordingly. There are variations: if the recipient denies receipt, the agent must prove with evidence or the principal's confirmation. If the principal confirms the agent, the agent can seek recourse from the principal for what he paid. The passage cites al-Quduri and al-Jami' with a preferred view from al-Bada'i'. A contemporary fatwa incident is discussed: when the commander and agent differ on whom the money was to be given, the agent's word is taken because they agreed on the original permission, making the agent a trustee. This is analogous to the mudaraba case. Other rulings: the agent is not compelled to perform the task unless it involves returning a deposit when the owner is absent. Agency with a fee: valid for collecting a deposit, but for collecting a debt it requires a specified time. General agency excludes divorce, manumission, gift, and charity according to the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
الأمين (al-amin): A trustee who is trusted with property; he is not liable for loss unless negligent.
الوكالة العامة (al-wakala al-'amma): General agency where the agent is authorized to act in all matters except those explicitly excluded (like divorce, manumission, etc.).
الأجر (al-ujr): Fee or payment for service; in agency, a fee contract is valid only if the task is well-defined in duration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi, which acts are NOT included in a general agency?
A) Sale and purchase.
B) Divorce, manumission, gift, and charity.
C) All financial transactions.
D) Litigation and debt collection.

Key Takeaway
The agent is a trustee with limited liability; general agency does not authorize divorce, manumission, gift, or charity without explicit permission.

Agent's Liability and Oaths in Disputes
Listing rulings on agent's liability and oath procedures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that an agent is a trustee (amīn) and apply the procedural rules for oaths in disputes between agent, principal, and third parties regarding payment and receipt.

Core Idea
The agent is a trustee: his word is accepted for his own discharge, but not for the receiver's receipt.

In Hanafi fiqh, the agent (wakīl) is considered a trustee (amīn) over the property in his hands, similar to a depositee. This lesson explores the implications of this status for liability and the rules for resolving disputes when claims of payment are contested. Understanding these rules is crucial for agency law and for adjudicating claims in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Agent's Legal Status
Recognize that the agent is treated as an amīn (trustee) regarding property entrusted to him. This means he is not liable for loss without negligence, and his word is accepted regarding his own discharge from liability.
The agent is like a depositee (mūdaʿ) in liability and discharge.
2
Distinguish Discharge from Receipt
In disputes over payment of a debt: if the agent claims he paid and the creditor denies receipt, the agent's word is accepted for his own discharge (barāʾa), but the creditor's word is accepted for non-receipt. Thus the debt remains owed to the creditor.
The agent is freed from liability to the principal, but the principal still owes the creditor.
3
Determine Who Takes the Oath
An oath is obligatory on one of the parties. The party believed by the principal does not swear; the one denied by the principal must swear. For example, if the principal denies the agent's claim of payment, the agent swears. If the principal believes the agent, the creditor swears.
The principal administers the oath to the party he denies.
4
Apply the Rules to Similar Cases
Extend the same principles to cases of deposit, stolen property, and debts: the agent's word is accepted only for his own liability, not for the receiver's receipt. If the receiver denies receipt, the receiver's word is taken with oath, and the agent must provide evidence or confirmation from the principal.
The agent cannot prove payment to the receiver without evidence or the principal's confirmation.
5
Examine the Fatwa Incident and Contrary Views
Analyze the fatwa incident where agent and principal differed on the intended recipient; the agent's word was accepted because both acknowledged the original permission. Note the difference of opinion regarding the agent's recourse if the principal believes him: al-Quduri allows recourse, while al-Jāmiʿ denies it, with the former being more appropriate.
This illustrates the principle that the agent remains a trustee as long as the original permission is agreed.

Source Text

الخامس في حكمها فمنه ثبوت ولاية التصرف الذي تناوله التوكيل، ومنه أن لا يوكل إلا بإذن أو تعميم، ومنه أنه أمين فيما في يده كالمودع فيضمن بما يضمن به المودع ويبرأ بما يبرأ به والقول قوله في دفع الضمان عن نفسه، فلو دفع له مالاً وقال اقضه فلاناً عن ديني فقال قضيته وكذبه صاحب الدين فالقول للوكيل في براءته وللدائن في عدم قبضه فلا يسقط دينه، ويجب اليمين على أحدهما فيحلف من كذبه الموكل دون من صدقه. وعلى هذا لو أمر المودع بدفعها إلى فلان فادعاه وكذبه فلان ولو كان المال مضموناً على رجل كالمغصوب في يد الغاصب أو الدين على الطالب فأمر الطالب أو المغصوب منه الرجل أن يدفعه إلى فلان فقال المأمور قد دفعت إليه وقال فلان ما قبضت فالقول قول فلان أنه لم يقبض ولم يصدق الوكيل على الدفع إلا ببينة أو بتصديق الموكل ولا يصدقان على القابض والقول له مع اليمين وللوكيل تحليف الموكل أنه ما يعلم أنه دفع ، فإن نكل سقط الضمان عنه. ولو لم يدفع إليه شيئاً وإنما أمره بقضاء دينه من ماله فادعاه وكذبه الطالب والموكل ولا بينة فالقول قولهما مع اليمين ويحلف الموكل على نفي العلم وإن صدقه الموكل دون الطالب رجع عليه بما ادعاه ويرجع الطالب عليه أيضاً بدينه ؛ ذكره القدوري. وفي الجامع : لا رجوع للوكيل على موكله ولو صدقه والأول أشبه كما في البدائع : ولو ادعى المودع أنه أمره بدفعها إلا فلان وكذبه صاحبها فالقول له أنه لم يأمره، وقد وقعت حادثة الفتوى حين تأليف هذا المحل دفع إلى آخر مالاً ليدفعه إلى آخر ثم اختلفا في تعيينه فقال الآمر أمرتك بدفعه إلى زيد فقال المأمور إلى عمرو وقد دفعت له فأجيب بأن القول قول الوكيل لأنهما اتفقا على أصل الإذن فكان أميناً ولهذا قال الزيلعي في آخر المضاربة : لو دفع إليه مالاً ثم اختلفا فقال الدافع مضاربة وقال المدفوع إليه وديعة فالقول للمدفوع إليه لأنهما اتفقا على الإذن ا هـ.
The fifth regarding its rulings: from them is the establishment of authority over the disposition which the appointment covers; from them is that he may not appoint a sub-agent except with permission or a general authorization; from them is that he is a trustee regarding what is in his hand like the depositee, so he is liable for what the depositee is liable for, and he becomes free from liability for what the depositee becomes free, and his word is taken regarding the removal of liability from himself. So if he gave him money and said: 'Pay it to so-and-so for my debt,' and he said 'I paid it,' and the debt owner denied him, then the word is the agent's regarding his own discharge, and the creditor's regarding non-receipt, so his debt does not fall. An oath is obligatory upon one of them; the one whom the principal denied swears, not the one he believed. Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage enumerates the fifth set of rulings (الخامس في حكمها) regarding agency. It first states three general principles: (1) the agent gains authority over the specific act delegated; (2) he cannot delegate further without permission or a general authorization; (3) the agent is a trustee (أمين) like a depositee, so his liability and discharge follow the rules of deposit, and his word is accepted regarding his own discharge from liability. The text then provides several detailed scenarios: when the agent claims he paid a debt but the creditor denies, the agent's word is taken for his own discharge, but the creditor's word is taken for non-receipt; an oath is required from one party, and the principal administers the oath to the one he denies. Similar rules apply to deposits, debts, and stolen property. A fatwa incident illustrates the principle: when the agent and the payee dispute the intended recipient, the agent's word is accepted because both acknowledge the original permission. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Zaylai's similar ruling in the context of mudarabah vs. deposit.

Key Terms
أمين (amīn): A trustee who is not liable for loss without negligence; the agent is considered an amin regarding property in his hands.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for compensation or guarantee; the agent's liability is similar to that of a depositee.
يمين (yamīn): Oath taken to affirm a claim or deny liability; used here to resolve disputes between agent and principal or creditor.
براءة (barāʾa): Discharge from liability; the agent's claim of payment results in his barāʾa from the principal.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; one appointed to act on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute where an agent claims he paid a debt but the creditor denies, whose word is accepted for the agent's discharge, and whose for the creditor's receipt?
A) The agent's word for both.
B) The agent's word for discharge, the creditor's word for receipt.
C) The creditor's word for both.
D) The principal's word for both.

Key Takeaway
The agent's status as amīn means his word is accepted for his own discharge, but the creditor's word is accepted for non-receipt, preserving the debt until actual payment is proven.

Agent's Obligation: When Compelled and When Hiring Requires a Time
Stating the agent's non-compulsion and conditions for hired agency in debt collection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the only task an agent is compelled to perform and identify the condition for a valid hiring of an agent for debt collection.

Core Idea
An agent is free to decline every task except returning a deposit; hiring for debt collection is void unless a time is fixed.

The Hanafi school establishes a general principle that an agent (wakil) is not compelled to execute the task for which he was appointed. He may refuse without liability. However, there is one exception: returning a deposit that has been accepted. If the principal is absent, the agent must return it. The passage also addresses the hired agency (ijara) for debt collection and litigation, ruling that without a specified time, the contract is invalid. This lesson unpacks these two important sub-rules of agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule and Its Exception
Read the first ruling: 'there is nothing upon him regarding doing what he was appointed for except in returning a deposit.' This establishes that the agent is not compelled for any act except returning a deposit. Recognize that this is the default: no compulsion.
The exception applies only when the agent has already accepted the deposit and the owner is absent.
2
Distinguish the Ruling for Returning a Deposit vs. Other Tasks
The passage gives an example: if the principal says 'give this garment to so-and-so' and the agent accepts, he is compelled to give it when the owner is absent. For all other tasks, he may refuse. Note the key difference: compulsion only in the case of returning a deposit.
This is because the deposit is an amanah that must be restored; delay could lead to loss.
3
Analyze the Ruling on Hiring for Debt Collection
The passage states that if someone hires an agent (gives him a wage) to collect a deposit (wadi'ah), it is valid. But if hired to collect a debt (dayn), it is invalid unless a specific time is stated. Understand that the condition for validity is the specification of a time period.
The reason for the time requirement is to avoid gharar (uncertainty) in the extent of work for debt collection, as debts may be collected at once or in installments over an unknown period.
4
Apply the Rule to Litigation Agency
The passage extends the same ruling to an agent appointed for litigation (taqadi) and for court representation (khusumah). Both require a specified time for the hiring to be valid. Note that this is parallel to the debt collection case.
Litigation is uncertain in duration, so a time limit removes uncertainty in the wage.
5
Synthesize the Two Key Rulings
Combine the two rulings: (1) The agent is generally not compelled to act, except to return a deposit. (2) If the agency is for a wage (ijara), and the task is debt collection or litigation, a time must be specified or the contract is void. Practice stating these rules concisely.
These rulings protect the agent from undue burden and prevent exploitative contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحكامه أنه لا عليه في فعل ما وكل به إلا في رد وديعة بأن قال ادفع هذا الثوب إلى فلان فقبله وغاب الأمر يجبر المأمور على دفعه، فأما سائر الأشياء فلا يجب عليه التنفيذ؛ كذا في المحيط وتمامه في فوائدنا . ومنها ما في البزازية وكله بقبض وديعته وجعل له الأجر صح وإن وكله بقبض دينه وجعل له أجراً لا يصح إلا إذا وقت مدة معلومة، وكذا الوكيل بالتقاضي إن وقت جاز وكذا الوكيل بالخصومة كذا في الولوالجية.
And from his rulings: there is nothing upon him regarding doing what he was appointed for except in returning a deposit, such as if he said: 'Give this garment to so-and-so,' and he accepted it and the matter is absent, the commanded one is compelled to give it. As for other things, he is not obligated to execute; likewise in al-Muhit and its completion in our benefits. And from them is what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: if he appointed him to collect his deposit and made an wage for him, it is valid; but if he appointed him to collect his debt and made an wage for him, it is invalid unless he specified a known time. Likewise, the agent for litigation (by appointment) if he specified a time, it is permissible; likewise the agent for litigation (for court) as in al-Walwalijiyyah.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage clarifies two additional rulings of agency. First, the agent is generally not compelled to carry out the task for which he was appointed; he may refuse without liability. The exception is when the task is to return a deposit (وديعة) that has been accepted; in that case, the agent is compelled to return it if the owner is absent. Second, the passage discusses hiring an agent (with a fee) for specific tasks: collecting a deposit can be validly hired; collecting a debt for a fee is invalid unless a specific time period is agreed upon. Similarly, appointing an agent to pursue litigation (التقاضي) is only valid if a time is specified; the same applies to appointing an agent for litigation (الخصومة) in court.

Key Terms
رد وديعة (radd wadīʿah): Returning a deposit; this is the only task the agent is compelled to perform.
أجر (ajr): Wage or fee for the agent's service; relevant when the agency is hired.
تقاضي (taqāḍī): Litigation or pursuing a debt through legal means; agency for this requires a specified time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a hired agency (with a wage) for debt collection valid?
A) Always, as long as the debt is certain.
B) Only if a specific time period is agreed upon.
C) Only if the agent is also appointed to collect deposits.
D) It is never valid to hire an agent for debt collection.

Key Takeaway
An agent is generally free to decline any task except returning a deposit; hiring an agent for debt collection or litigation is only valid if a specific time is fixed.

Invalid Conditions in Agency and Its Non-Binding Nature
Ruling that agency is not invalidated by invalid conditions and that it is non-binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that agency is not invalidated by invalid conditions and explain why the principal can revoke the agency at any time.

Core Idea
Agency survives invalid conditions; the principal retains the power to revoke anytime.

This passage presents two fundamental rulings of agency (wakalah) in the Hanafi school. First, the validity of the agency is not affected by the inclusion of invalid conditions (shurūṭ fāsidah); such conditions are simply void and ignored. Second, by default, agency is a non-binding contract (ghayr lāzim), meaning the principal may dismiss the agent at will without cause. However, there is an exception to this general rule, which will be mentioned later in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage lists two distinct rulings: (1) agency is not invalidated by invalid conditions, and (2) agency is non-binding.
2
Understand the effect of invalid conditions
Invalid conditions are void but do not affect the validity of the agency contract. The condition is ignored, and the contract remains effective.
Example: a condition that the agent must not sell to a specific person is invalid; the agency stands.
3
Analyze the non-binding nature
The principal can revoke the agency at any time, reflecting the principle that agency is a contract of trust and can be terminated at will.
This is the default rule; exceptions exist (e.g., when agency becomes binding due to third-party rights).
4
Note the exception
The passage mentions an exception to the non-binding rule, which will be discussed later (likely agency that becomes binding when the agent has already performed or when a third party right is involved).
For now, understand the general rule: agency is ghayr lāzim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحكامها أنها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة ولا يصح شرط الخيار فيها كما في الخانية. السادس في صفتها وهو عدم اللزوم فله أن يعزله متى شاء إلا فيما سنذكره آخرها .
And from its rulings: it is not invalidated by invalid conditions, and the stipulation of an option is not valid in it, as in al-Khaniyyah. The sixth is regarding its nature: it is non-binding, so he may dismiss him whenever he wishes, except in what we will mention at its end.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings. First, agency is not invalidated by the inclusion of invalid conditions (شروط فاسدة); for example, a condition that contradicts the nature of the contract. Additionally, stipulating an option (خيار) to cancel the agency is invalid. Second, the sixth ruling (السادس) describes the nature of agency: it is non-binding (عدم اللزوم), meaning the principal can revoke the agent's authority at any time without cause. The exception to this is mentioned later in the text (regarding certain cases where agency becomes binding).

Key Terms
شروط فاسدة (shurūṭ fāsidah): Invalid conditions that do not render the agency void; they are ignored.
عدم اللزوم (ʿadam al-luzūm): Non-binding nature; the principal may revoke the agency at will.
شرط الخيار (sharṭ al-khiyār): Stipulation of an option to cancel the contract; in agency, such a stipulation is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about an agency contract that includes an invalid condition?
A) The agency becomes void.
B) The agency remains valid but the condition must be followed.
C) The agency remains valid and the condition is ignored.
D) The agency is suspended until the condition is removed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that agency contracts are resilient: invalid conditions are ignored, and the principal can revoke the agent at will.

Validity, Definition, and Conditions of the Principal in Agency
Establishing the validity of agency with proofs, defining it, and stating conditions of the principal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three proofs for the validity of agency, explain the definition of tawkīl, and identify the conditions of the principal, including the dispute about general agency including divorce.

Core Idea
Agency is valid by Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma; the principal must own the right to do the act himself.

Agency (tawkīl) is a foundational concept in Islamic commercial and family law. This lesson establishes its validity through scriptural and communal proofs, defines it precisely, and clarifies who can be a principal. A key intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of general agency is also examined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proofs for the validity of agency
The passage begins with 'صح التوكيل' and cites three proofs: the Quranic verse about the People of the Cave (18:19), the Prophet's appointment of Hakim ibn Hizam, and Ijma. Note that the Hanafi school accepts 'shar' man qablana' as a source under certain conditions.
The verse is considered a proof because Allah narrates it without criticism and no abrogation is known.
2
Understand the definition of agency
Agency is defined as 'delegating disposition to another' or 'establishing another in one's own place in disposition.' It requires the disposition to be permissible and known; if unknown, the minimal disposition (preservation) is established.
The definition distinguishes agency from mere authorization (tahkīm).
3
Distinguish general vs. specific agency and the divorce dispute
General agency uses phrases like 'You are my agent in everything.' The preferred Hanafi view (al-Sadr al-Shahid) includes all permissible acts, even divorce. Abu al-Layth excludes divorce and waqf. Know the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): divorce by a general agent is valid under the preferred view.
The fatwa today follows al-Sadr al-Shahid.
4
Analyze the condition of the principal: capacity
The principal must 'own' the act (mimman yamlikuhu). Thus, a madman or non-discerning child cannot appoint an agent. A discerning child can appoint for beneficial acts (e.g., accepting a gift) without guardian permission, but not for harmful acts. For mixed acts (sale, lease), if permitted to trade, appointment is valid; otherwise, it requires guardian approval.
This condition also includes the father and executor over a child's wealth.
5
Examine special cases: slave, mukatib, apostate, and objections
A restricted slave cannot appoint; a permitted slave and mukatib can. An apostate's agency is suspended (Abu Hanifa) or effective (Sahibayn). Also, a Muslim can appoint a dhimmi to sell wine/pork? The answer: the Muslim owns the right to engage in disposition in principle, but the specific sale is prohibited; the agency itself is valid in form but the sale is invalid. A similar objection about a slave not owning the right to appoint for his own marriage is answered briefly.
These cases illustrate the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (صح التوكيل أي تفويض التصرف إلى الغير بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع قال تعالى حكاية عن أصحاب الكهف فابعثوا أحدكم بورقكم هذه إلى المدينة [الكهف: ١٩] وكان البعث منهم بطريق الوكالة وشرع من قبلنا شرع لنا إذا قصه الله تعالى ورسوله من غير إنكار ولم يظهر نسخة ووكل عليه السلام حكيم بن حزام بشراء أضحيته وانعقد الإجماع عليه وهو عام وخاص، فالثاني ،ظاهر والأول نحو أن يقول ما صنعت من شيء فهو جائز، أنت وكيلي في كل شيء جائز، أمرك علي ملك جميع أنواع التصرفات من البيع والشراء والهبة والصدقة والتقاضي وغير ذلك، ولو طلق امرأته جاز. قال الصدر الشهيد وبه يفتي حتى يتبين خلافه. واختار أبو الليث أنه لو طلق أو وقف لم يجز ؛ كذا في الولوالجية. وفي البزازية : ما حكمت فجائز تحكيم لا توكيل. وقدمنا فتوى قاضيخان أنه يختص بالمعاوضات قوله : (وهو إقامة الغير مقام نفسه في التصرف أي الجائز المعلوم حتى إن التصرف إذا لم يكن معلوماً ثبت أدنى التصرفات وهو الحفظ فيما إذا قال وكلتك بمالي قوله : (ممن يملكه) لا يعقل مطلقاً أي ذلك التصرف بيان للشرط في الموكل فلا يصح توكيل مجنون وصبي وصبي يعقل بنحو طلاق وعتاق وهبة وصدقة من التصرفات الضارة فيصح توكيله بالنافعة بلا إذن وليه كقبول الهبة وأما ما تردد بين ضرر ونفع كالبيع والإجارة فإن كان مأذوناً في التجارة صح توکیله مطلقاً وإلا توقف على إجازة وليه ولا يصح توكيل عبد محجور وصح من مأذون ومكاتب، وأما توكيل المرتد فموقوف إن أسلم نفذ وإلا بأن قتل أو مات أو لحق بطل وقالا : نافذ. وشمل قوله ممن يملكه الأب والوصي في مال الصبي فلهما أن بكل ما يفعلانه، وأورد على هذا الشرط توكيل المسلم ذمياً ببيع خمر أو خنزير وتوكيل المحرم عنده ولا يملكه الموكل . وأجيب بأنه يملكه بأصل التصرف الصيد فإنه ببيع عنده . الحلال صحيح يوكلا وإن امتنع بعارض النهي، ويرد عليه العبد المأذون في تزويج نفسه لا يملك التوكيل كما في عليه ويجيب عنه اهـ.
His saying: (Appointment is valid) i.e., delegating disposition to another by the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma. Allah said, narrating about the People of the Cave: 'So send one of you with this silver of yours to the city' [18:19]. Their sending was by way of agency, and the law of those before us is law for us if Allah and His Messenger narrate it without rejection and no abrogation appears. And he, peace be upon him, appointed Hakim ibn Hizam to buy his sacrificial animal, and Ijma has been established upon it. It is general and specific; the second is apparent. The first is like his saying: 'Whatever you do is permissible,' 'You are my agent in everything permissible,' 'Your command over me encompasses all types of dispositions like selling, buying, giving, charity, litigation, etc.,' and if he divorces his wife, it is valid. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: and by this fatwa is given until its contrary appears. Abu al-Layth chose that if he divorces or waqfs, it is not valid; similarly in al-Walwalijiyyah. His saying: (It is establishing another in his own place in the disposition) i.e., the permissible known disposition, so that if the disposition is not known, the least disposition is established, which is preservation, when he says 'I appoint you over my wealth.' His saying: (From one who owns it) i.e., he does not understand absolutely, i.e., that disposition; an exposition of the condition in the principal. So appointment is not valid for a madman or a child, and a child who understands regarding harmful dispositions like divorce, emancipation, gift, charity is not valid; so his appointment for beneficial dispositions is valid without the permission of his guardian, like accepting a gift. As for dispositions that are between harm and benefit, like sale and lease, if he is permitted to trade, his appointment is valid absolutely; otherwise it depends on the guardian's approval. Appointment of a restricted slave is not valid; it is valid from a permitted one and a mukatib. As for appointment of an apostate, it is suspended; if he becomes Muslim, it is effective; otherwise, if he is killed, dies, or joins (the enemy), it becomes void; and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: effective. Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the validity of agency (صح التوكيل) through the Qur'an (story of the People of the Cave), the Sunnah (the Prophet appointing Hakim ibn Hizam to buy a sacrificial animal), and Ijma. It defines agency as 'delegating disposition to another' and notes that it can be general ('You are my agent in everything') or specific; the general form allows all dispositions including divorce according to the preferred view. The passage then states the condition for the principal: he must be legally capable of the act he delegates. Thus, appointment by an insane person or a child is invalid for harmful acts; for beneficial acts, a discerning child's appointment is valid. For acts between harm and benefit (like sale), if the child is permitted to trade, his appointment is valid; otherwise it requires guardian approval. The passage also discusses the appointment of a restricted slave (invalid), a permitted slave or mukatib (valid), and an apostate (suspended according to Abu Hanifa; immediately effective according to the Sahibayn). The condition 'mimman yamlikuhu' (from one who owns it) includes fathers and executors over a child's wealth. An objection is raised regarding a Muslim appointing a dhimmi to sell wine or pork (which the Muslim does not own), but the answer is that the Muslim owns the original right to engage in the disposition (e.g., selling permissible things) but is prohibited from this specific transaction; agency in such cases is valid in principle but the sale itself is invalid. Another objection about a slave not owning the right to appoint an agent for his own marriage is answered (briefly).

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkīl): Appointment of an agent; delegating one's own disposition to another.
تفويض التصرف (tafwīḍ al-taṣarruf): Delegating the authority to conduct legal acts; synonym for tawkīl.
شرع من قبلنا (sharʿ man qablanā): The law of previous prophets, considered valid for us if narrated in the Qur'an or Sunnah without abrogation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Agency is valid by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma; the principal must be capable of the act, and a general agency includes all permissible acts including divorce according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Capacity of a Discerning Minor in Wakalah: Understanding Ghubn
Commentary on the ضابط of wakalah and the child's understanding of ghubn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for a minor's valid agency and apply it to distinguish between valid and invalid transactions.

Core Idea
A minor's agency is valid only if he can practically distinguish gross cheating from slight.

This lesson expands upon the ضابط of wakalah from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The key question is what capacity a minor must have to act as an agent. The Hanafi school requires a child to understand the difference between gross and slight cheating based on practical market knowledge, not formal fiqh training.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject of Agency
The passage states that the subject of agency is 'everything that a person can contract by himself'. This is a ضابط (controlling description), not a strict definition. Understand that it covers all permissible transactions.
The ضابط is broad; exceptions like wine do not invalidate it.
2
Analyze the Condition of Ghubn Knowledge
The core dispute is whether a discerning minor must know the difference between gross and slight cheating for his agency to be valid. The majority holds that this practical knowledge is required.
The knowledge is practical, not theoretical – knowing market values, not fiqh definitions.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Some Hanafis (e.g., in al-Ya'qubiyyah) argue that requiring such knowledge is problematic because it is not commonly attained. The author refutes this by citing practical examples and counter-arguments from al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah.
The dispute is about the degree of knowledge, not the principle of capacity.
4
Apply the Ruling to Scenarios
Use the condition to test transactions: If a child cannot tell that five dirhams is too much for something worth ten, his sale is invalid. If he can, it is valid.
The condition is individualized based on the child's actual understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
الربح للاحتراز عن بيع المكره والهازل فإنه لا يقع عن الآمر ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأنه لا حاجة إلى اشتراط عقلية الغبن الفاحش من اليسير الجواز بيع الوكيل عند الإمام بما قل وكثر. نعم إن قيد عليه أن لا يبيعه بغبن فاحش اشتراط، وأما تفسير القصد للاحتراز عن بيع والمكره فخارج عن المقصود لأن الكلام الآن في صحة الوكالة لا في صحة الوكيل ولذا بيع الهازل تركه المصنف. وفي الواقعات الحسامية الوكيل إذا اختلط عقله بشراب نبيذ ويعرف الشراء والقبض جاز على الموكل شراؤه . ولو اختلط ببنج ويعرف الشراء لم يجز وهو بمنزلة المعتوه اه قوله : (بكل ما يعقد بنفسه بيان لضابط الموكل فيه وليس حداً فلا يرد عليه أن المسلم لا الخمر ولا يملك توكيل الذمي به لأن إبطال القواعد بإبطال الطرد لا العكس. ولا بيع يملك

Book of Agency. Profit is for caution against the sale of the coerced and the jester, for it does not occur on behalf of the principal, end. Then after writing that I saw in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah he said: his saying 'something that no one can know' is denied, for we see many who know that without engaging in fiqh, but by hearing from trustworthy people and frequent direct dealing. Then the one capable of something may take the place of that thing, as previously discussed in the topics of rejecting the testimony of a blind person in this book. As for what we are in, capability of knowledge by intellect exists in the child who is our subject, so reflect, end. I say: it is apparent that what they mean is that he knows that five in what is worth ten, for example, is gross cheating, and that one in it is slight. For whoever does not perceive the difference between them is not sane, like a child to whom a man gives a slippers and takes his garment, and he is happy with it and does not know that he is cheated in that, his transaction is not valid at all. It is valid from children.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the definition of agency (wakalah) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author first discusses the phrase 'profit is for caution against the sale of the coerced and the jester' from the matn. He explains that this is to exclude cases where the sale does not occur on behalf of the principal. Then he raises a consideration (نظر) about stipulating the understanding of gross cheating (ghubn fahish) from slight (yasir) for the validity of the agent's sale according to Imam Abu Hanifa, who permits sale whether the profit is small or large. However, if the principal explicitly prohibits selling with gross cheating, then it becomes a restriction. The author then comments on the phrase 'with everything that he can contract by himself' (بكل ما يعقد بنفسه). He clarifies that this is a ضابط (controlling description) for what can be the subject of agency, not a proper definition (حد). Therefore, it is not invalidated by exceptions like a Muslim cannot be authorized to sell wine, nor can a dhimmi authorize a Muslim to sell wine, because the ضابط works as a positive condition, not an absolute rule. The text then delves into a dispute about the condition that a minor agent (الصبي العاقل) must know the difference between gross and slight cheating. Some argue this is unnecessary because such knowledge is obtained only through fiqh study, which is not required for a child's valid agency. Others respond that the child's understanding is practical, not theoretical; he must know market values, not legal definitions. The passage concludes with examples supporting the condition: if a child cannot tell that five dirhams is too much for something worth ten, he is not considered sane enough for valid transactions. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on practical rationality in contractual capacity.

Key Terms
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fāḥish): A transaction where the price deviates significantly from market value (e.g., paying ten for something worth five).
الصبي العاقل (al-ṣabī al-ʿāqil): A discerning minor who can understand the nature of transactions and distinguish between beneficial and harmful deals.
ضابط (ḍābiṭ): A controlling description that indicates permissibility but is not a strict definition (حد), so exceptions do not invalidate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum requirement for a minor's agency to be valid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) The minor must have reached puberty
B) The minor must know the difference between gross and slight cheating
C) The minor must be able to read and write
D) The minor must have the permission of his guardian

Key Takeaway
The discerning minor's agency requires practical knowledge of gross cheating; without it, his transactions are invalid.

Testing the Maxim: Dhimmi Appointing Muslim to Sell Wine
Testing the ضابط with the case of a dhimmi authorizing a Muslim to sell wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi legal maxim regarding agency ('every contract one can contract by himself') is defended against apparent counterexamples and apply the rule that the maxim concerns achieving the contract, not personal performance.

Core Idea
A maxim is valid despite exceptions if the core principle remains intact.

This lesson examines the scrutiny of a key legal maxim (ضابط) in Hanafi agency law: 'Every contract a person can contract by himself, he can authorize another to do.' The commentator in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tests this maxim with potential counterexamples—such as a dhimmi appointing a Muslim to sell wine—and defends it by clarifying that the maxim means the ability to achieve the contract via some means, not necessarily personally. Through this exercise, you will learn how Hanafi jurists protect general principles from being invalidated by exceptional cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim and its Initial Scope
Identify the maxim: كل عقد يملكه بنفسه يملك التوكيل به. Understand its apparent meaning: personal ability to contract.
2
Examine the First Objection: Dhimmi and Wine
Consider the case: a dhimmi owns wine and can contract its sale himself, but can he authorize a Muslim to sell it? The objection is that the dhimmi cannot personally execute the sale, but the response is that the maxim is about achieving the contract, not personal performance.
The dhimmi can achieve the sale by authorizing the Muslim, so the maxim holds.
3
Analyze the Commentator's Defense
The commentator explains that the maxim does not require personal performance; it requires that the person can achieve the contract in some manner. The dhimmi can achieve the sale by having a Muslim sell his wine. Therefore, the maxim is satisfied.
4
Consider Further Objections and Responses
Additional objections: agent sub-delegation, father buying for minor, borrowing (istiqrad). For each, the principle is defended by noting that the maxim refers to the principal himself (لنفسه) or by citing fatwa works that allow agency for borrowing.
The borrowing case is resolved by al-Khaniyyah: agency for borrowing is valid if the agent attributes the loan to the principal.
5
Derive the Correct Application
Conclude that the maxim remains valid; apparent exceptions are either not true exceptions or are separately addressed by other rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا) يمكن طرده الخ) لعله ولا البحر الرائق ج٧/ م ١٦
قال في التبيين: ولا يرد عليه توكيل الذمي مسلماً ببيع خمره وهو يملكه لأنه يملك التوصل به بتوكيل الذمي به فصدق الضابط لأنه لم يقل كل عقد يملكه يملك توكيل كل أحد به بل التوصل به في الجملة، وإنما يرد عليه توكيل الوكيل بلا إذن وتعميم فإنه يملك العقد الذي وكل به ولا يملك التوكيل. وأجابوا بأن المراد لنفسه لكن يرد عليه الأب والجد يملكان شراء مال ولده الصغير ولا يملكان التوكيل به كما في السراج الوهاج والاستقراض فإنه يباشره بنفسه لنفسه ولا يملك التوكيل به فيقع للوكيل؛ كذا ذكر الشارح. ولم يجب عنه والجواب منع عدم صحته به لما في الخانية : إن وكل بالاستقراض فإن أضاف الوكيل الاستقراض إلى الموكل كان للموكل وإلا كان للوكيل ا هـ . وفي البزازية : استقرض منه ألفاً وأمره أن يعطيه رسوله فلاناً

يصح یرد.

His saying: (and) it cannot be fully applied etc.) perhaps it is 'and' [in the matn]. He said in al-Tabyin: and it is not objected to it [that a dhimmi authorizes a Muslim to sell wine while he owns it because he can achieve the objective by the dhimmi authorizing him to do so, so the ضابط is true because he did not say 'every contract he can contract he can authorize anyone to do so' but rather 'can achieve it in general.' It is only objected that an agent can delegate without permission and generalization, for he owns the contract he was authorized for but does not own authorization. And they answered that the meaning is for himself. But it is objected that the father and grandfather own buying the property of their minor child but do not own authorization for it, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj; and borrowing (istiqrad) he does it himself for himself and does not own authorization for it, so it falls to the agent; thus mentioned the commentator. And he did not answer it. And the answer is to deny its lack of validity because in al-Khaniyyah: if he authorized for borrowing, then if the agent attributes the borrowing to the principal, it is for the principal, otherwise for the agent, end. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: he borrowed a thousand from him and ordered him to give it to his messenger so-and-so from... and twenty from... valid? reply. He said: it invalidates its full application.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the ضابط of agency. The author raises an objection: does the ضابط 'with everything he can contract by himself' apply when a dhimmi authorizes a Muslim to sell wine? The dhimmi can contract for wine himself, but can he authorize someone else to sell it? The response is that the ضابط does not require that he can authorize any person for any contract; rather, it means he can achieve the contract by some means. Here, the dhimmi can achieve the sale by authorizing a Muslim to sell his wine (since the Muslim can validly sell it). Therefore, the ضابط is not violated. The author then notes another possible objection: an agent (وكيل) cannot sub-delegate without permission, even though he can contract the original act. The response is that the ضابط is meant 'for himself' (لنفسه). A further objection is raised: a father or grandfather can buy property for his minor child directly but cannot authorize another to do so (as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj); similarly, borrowing (استقراض) is done directly and cannot be delegated. The commentator then provides a response from al-Khaniyyah and al-Bazzaziyyah, stating that agency for borrowing is valid under certain conditions: if the agent attributes the borrowing to the principal, the loan is for the principal; otherwise for the agent. This shows that the original objection does not hold.

Key Terms
الاستقراض (al-istiqrāḍ): The act of borrowing money; the principal asks the agent to borrow on his behalf.
توكيل الوكيل (tawkīl al-wakīl): The agent delegating his authority to another without the principal's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He says the dhimmi cannot contract wine sale at all.
C) He says the maxim only applies to Muslims.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
A legal maxim in Hanafi fiqh is not invalidated by apparent exceptions; it is understood according to its purpose and scope.
