Who is Believed: Agent or Creditor? Agency for Debt Payment
Further commentary on agency for debt payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whom to believe when an agent claims payment and the principal and creditor deny, and explain the reasoning behind the distinction.

Core Idea
In agency for debt payment, the agent's oath discharges him, but the creditor's oath preserves the debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical dispute: an agent given money to pay a debt claims he paid, but both the principal and the creditor deny it. Who is believed? The answer depends on what is at stake: the agent's personal liability vs. the creditor's right to the debt. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that protects both parties by accepting each party's statement under oath regarding their own interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The principal gives money to the agent to pay a debt. The agent claims he paid the creditor. Both the principal and the creditor deny the agent's claim. So the agent's statement is disputed by both.
The principal denies the agent paid (so agent may still owe principal). The creditor denies receiving payment (so debt may still be owed to creditor).
2
Determine the object of each claim
The agent seeks discharge from liability to the principal (براءة نفسه). The creditor seeks to deny receipt (إنكاره القبض), which protects his right to the debt. The principal's denial affects his relationship with the agent.
Each claim concerns a different legal relationship: agent-principal (discharge) and agent-creditor (payment).
3
Apply the principle: each party's statement is accepted in their own interest
The Hanafi ruling accepts the agent's statement (with oath) regarding his own discharge, and accepts the creditor's statement (with oath) regarding non-receipt. This means: the agent is free from liability to the principal, but the creditor can still claim the debt from the principal (since the principal is still liable to the creditor).
The agent's oath protects him from the principal's claim. The creditor's oath protects his right against the principal. The principal may have to pay the creditor again but can seek recourse from the agent if the agent lied (though the oath makes it difficult).
4
Note the authority and evidence
The passage cites Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah as the source for this ruling. This is a later Hanafi fatwa collection, indicating the mufta bihi (operative) position for fatwa.
The text does not provide a primary proof (Quran or hadith) but relies on the authority of later Hanafi scholarship.
5
Consider the wisdom of the ruling
The ruling balances the interests: the agent is not forced to pay twice (he is discharged from the principal), but the creditor's right is protected (he can still claim from the principal). This reflects the Hanafi preference for preserving rights while preventing unjust enrichment.
Some schools might give more weight to the agent's statement entirely, but the Hanafi view splits the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدوري قوله: (فمن مسائله قالوا لو وكله بقضاء الدين أي وكله بأن يدفع الوكيل من مال نفسه إلى دائن الموكل، وكذا في المسألة الآتية عن كتاب الحوالة، أما لو دفع إليه دراهم وقال له اقض بها ديني الذي لزيد فادعى الوكيل الدفع إلى زيد الدائن وكذبه كل من الموكل والدائن فالقول للوكيل في براءة نفسه بيمينه، والقول للدائن في إنكاره القبض ييمينه أيضاً كما في فتاوى قارئ الهداية قوله : (لا المأمور على الأمر أي لا يرجع بما قضاه بمال نفسه قوله : ولو قال لا تبع إلا بمحضر فلان الخ) قال في التتارخانية في أواخر الفصل الحادي عشر عازياً للمحيط : نوع آخر فيما إذا حصل التوكيل
Al-Quduri: His saying: (Among its issues, they said: if he appointed him to pay a debt) meaning he appointed him to pay from his own money to the principal's creditor. Similarly, in the issue coming from Kitab al-Hawalah. But if he gave him dirhams and said, 'Pay my debt to Zaid with this,' and the agent claimed payment to Zaid the creditor, and both the principal and the creditor denied him, then the agent's statement regarding his own discharge is accepted with his oath, and the creditor's statement denying receipt is accepted with his oath as well, as in Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah. His saying: (لا المأمور على الأمر) meaning he does not recourse with what he paid from his own money. His saying: (And if he said, 'Do not sell except in the presence of so-and-so' etc.) He said in al-Tatar Khaniyyah at the end of the eleventh chapter, citing al-Muhit: Another type of case regarding when agency occurs.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on al-Quduri's matn. It explains that if the principal gives money to the agent to pay a specific debt, and the agent claims to have paid but the principal and creditor both deny, the agent's statement regarding his own liability is accepted with his oath, meaning he is discharged from liability to the principal. However, the creditor's denial of receipt is also accepted, meaning the debt remains. The agent who pays from his own money cannot recourse against the principal if the payment is not effective. Additionally, there is a reference to a case from al-Muhit about the prohibition of selling except in the presence of a certain person.

Key Terms
قدوري (Quduri): Reference to Imam al-Quduri, author of the matn Mukhtasar al-Quduri, a foundational Hanafi text.
فتاوى قارئ الهداية (Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah): A collection of fatwas by a commentator of al-Hidayah.
براءة نفسه (bara'at nafsihi): Discharge of oneself from liability. Here, the agent's claim that he has fulfilled his duty, freeing him from any claim by the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Limited Authority of an Agent for Collecting a Debt
Listing the powers and limitations of an agent for collecting debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the powers of an agent for collecting a debt from an agent for sale, and apply the rule that the agent for collection cannot grant acquittance or take a pledge.

Core Idea
An agent to collect a debt can receive payment and discharge the debtor but cannot waive the debt or take a pledge.

When you appoint someone to collect a debt on your behalf, their authority is not unlimited. The Hanafi school carefully defines what the agent can and cannot do, ensuring the debtor's discharge is valid while protecting the principal's rights. This lesson explores the precise boundaries of such an agency, including key differences from an agent who sells goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core function of the agent for collection
Recognize that the agent is appointed solely to receive payment from the debtor. His statement about receipt, loss, and delivery is accepted, and the debtor is discharged.
This differs from the agent for sale who has broader authority.
2
List the agent's permitted actions
The agent may receive payment, collect part of the debt, take a guarantor from the debtor, and if both owe each other, set-off occurs automatically.
He cannot unilaterally waive the debt or accept less payment.
3
Identify the prohibited actions
The agent cannot grant acquittance (ibrā'), make a gift, take a pledge, accept a hawala (transfer of debt), or impose conditions that alter the debt's terms.
These actions exceed his limited mandate.
4
Compare with the agent for sale
An agent for sale has full authority (including taking pledges, granting acquittance) because his task involves selling property, whereas the collection agent's role is narrower.
This contrast appears in the passage to clarify the limits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني أعني الوكيل بقبض الدين فيقبل قوله في قبضه وضياعه ودفعه إلى الموكل ويبرأ الغريم ولو كان ممن لا تقبل شهادته للوكيل بخلاف إقراره بقبض الطالب، ولو وجب على الوكيل بالقبض مثله لمديون موكله وقعت المقاصة وكان الوكيل مديون الموكل. ولا يملك الوكيل بقبضه الإبراء والهبة وأخذ الرهن وملك أخذ الكفيل بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع حيث ملك الكل، وليس للوكيل بالقبض قبول الحوالة، ويصح التوكيل بالقبض والقضاء بلا رضا الخصم ولا ينعزل بموت المطلوب وينعزل بموت الطالب، فلو زعم الوكيل قبضه وتسليمه إلى الطالب حال حياته لم يصدق بلا حجة، فإن احتال الطالب بالمال بعد التوكيل على إنسان ليس للوكيل أن يطالب المحيل ،والمحتال فلو توى المال على المحال عليه وعاد الدين على المحيل فالوكيل يملك الطلب ولو كان بالمال كفيل أو أخذ الطالب كفيلاً بعد التوكيل ليس للوكيل أن يتقاضى الكفيل وللوكيل بالقبض قبض بعضه إلا إذا نص على أن لا يقبض إلا الكل معاً . ا هـ ما في البزازية والحاصل أن الوكيل بقبض الدين يخالف الوكيل بالبيع وقبض الثمن في مسائل فلو كفل الوكيل بقبض الثمن المشتري صحت، ولو كفل الوكيل بالبيع لم تصح كما في الخانية. وتقبل شهادة الوكيل بقبض الدين به على المديون في شهادات البزازية بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع ولو باع الوكيل وقبض الثمن ثم رد المبيع بعيب بعد ما دفع الثمن للموكل فللمشتري مطالبة الوكيل بخلاف الوكيل بقبض الثمن لا مطالبة عليه كما في القنية. ولا يصح إبراء الوكيل بالقبض ولا حطه ولا أخذه الرهن ولا بشرط ما يجب اعتباره وما لا يجب
As for the second [type], i.e., the agent for collecting a debt: his statement is accepted regarding his receipt, its loss, and its delivery to the principal, and the debtor is discharged, even if the agent's testimony would not be accepted [in court]. This is contrary to his acknowledgement of receipt of the [debt from the] claimant. And if a similar amount is due from the agent for collection to the debtor of his principal, a set-off occurs and the agent becomes a debtor of the principal. The agent for collection does not have the authority to grant acquittance, to gift, to take a pledge, [but] he has the authority to take a guarantor, unlike the agent for sale where he has all [these powers]. The agent for collection may not accept a hawāla (transfer of debt). It is valid to appoint an agent for collection and payment without the consent of the opponent, and he is not dismissed by the death of the [debtor], but is dismissed by the death of the claimant. If the agent claims that he collected and delivered to the claimant during his lifetime, he is not believed without proof. If the claimant, after the agency, transfers the money to someone else via hawāla, the agent may not demand from the transferor (muḥīl) nor the transferee (muḥtāl). If the money is lost with the transferee and the debt returns to the transferor, the agent may demand [from the transferor]. If there is a guarantor for the money, or if the claimant takes a guarantor after the agency, the agent may not demand from the guarantor. The agent for collection may collect part [of the debt] unless it is stipulated that he may not collect except the whole at once. End of what is in al-Bazzāziyya. The summary is that the agent for collecting a debt differs from the agent for sale and collection of price in several issues: if the agent for collection of price stands surety for the buyer, it is valid; if the agent for sale stands surety, it is not valid, as in al-Khāniyya. The testimony of the agent for collection of debt is accepted against the debtor in [cases of] testimonies in al-Bazzāziyya, contrary to the agent for sale. If the agent sold and collected the price, then the sold item was returned due to a defect after he had paid the price to the principal, the buyer may demand from the agent; contrary to the agent for collection of price—no demand upon him, as in al-Quniyya. The acquittance by the agent for collection is not valid, nor reduction, nor taking a pledge, nor a condition requiring what should or should not be considered.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage details the legal position of an agent appointed to collect a debt (وكيل بقبض الدين). The agent's statement about receipt, loss, and delivery is accepted, and the debtor is discharged, even if the agent would normally be an unacceptable witness. If the agent owes a similar amount to the principal's debtor, set-off occurs. The agent's powers are limited: he cannot grant acquittance, gift, or take a pledge, but he can take a guarantor. He cannot accept a hawala. The agency does not end with the debtor's death but ends with the claimant's death. If the agent claims collection and delivery during the claimant's lifetime, he is not believed without evidence. After the claimant transfers the debt via hawala, the agent's authority shifts. The agent may collect part unless restricted. The passage then compares the agent for debt with the agent for sale in several issues: guarantees, testimony, and liability upon return of sold item. Finally, it lists invalid actions for the agent for collection: granting acquittance, reduction, taking a pledge, or stipulating conditions.

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to collect a debt from the debtor on behalf of the principal.
إبراء (ibrā'): Acquittance or release from debt; agent for collection cannot do this.
حوالة (ḥawāla): Transfer of debt from debtor to a third party; agent for collection cannot accept it.
كفيل (kafīl): Guarantor; agent for collection can take a guarantor from the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can an agent appointed to collect a debt grant acquittance (ibrā') to the debtor?
A) Yes, as part of his authority.
B) No, he cannot.
C) Only if the principal authorizes him in writing.
D) Only for part of the debt.

Key Takeaway
An agent for collecting a debt has strictly limited powers: he can receive payment and discharge the debtor, but cannot forgive, gift, pledge, or transfer the debt without special authorization.

Conditions in Agency: The Three Categories
Categorizing conditions based on benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditions stipulated by a principal on an agent into three categories and determine whether the agent must observe them.

Core Idea
The agent must observe conditions that benefit the principal in every way, but may disregard harmful ones unless mixed with emphasis.

In agency, the principal may set conditions. But not all conditions are equally binding. The Hanafi fiqh classifies them into three types based on the benefit they provide. This lesson explores that classification and the role of emphasis (ta'kīd bi al-nafy).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and note the three types of conditions: fully beneficial, entirely harmful, and mixed (beneficial in some ways).
2
Determine obligation for each category
For fully beneficial conditions, the agent must follow regardless of emphasis. For entirely harmful conditions, the agent may disregard even if emphasized. For mixed conditions, emphasis matters: if emphasized, must follow; otherwise, may deviate.
Emphasis means using a negative phrase like 'except' or 'do not'.
3
Analyze the examples
Study the three examples given: sell with option (fully beneficial), sell on credit (harmful), sell in a specific market (mixed). Match each to the rule.
4
Apply the principle
Given a new scenario, identify the condition type and decide if the agent's action is valid.
Practice with the tasks below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذا النوع إن الموكل إذا شرط على الوكيل شرطاً مفيداً من كل وجه بأن كان ينفعه من كل وجه فإنه يجب على الوكيل مراعاة شرطه، أكده بالنفي أو لم يؤكده. بيانه فيما إذا قال بعه بخيار فباعه بغير خيار لا يجوز، وإن شرط في العقد شرطاً لا يفيد أصلاً بأن كان لا ينفعه بوجه بل يضره لا يجب على الوكيل مراعاته، أكده الموكل بالنفي أو لم يؤكده . بيانه فيما إذا قال بعه بألف نسيئة أو قال لا تبعه إلا بألف نسيئة فباعه بألف نقداً يجوز على الأمر، فإذا شرط شرطاً يفيد من وجه ولا يفيد من وجه بأن كان ينفع من وجه ولا ينفع من وجه، إن أكده بالنفي يجب مراعاته، وإن لم يؤكده لا يجب مراعاته بيانه فيما إذا قال بعه في سوق كذا فباعه في سوق آخر، فإن لم يؤكده بالنفي بأن لم يقل إلا في سوق كذا فباعه في سوق آخر ينفذ على الأمر، وإن أكده بالنفي لا ينفذ على الآمر اهـ. وتمام التفاريع فيها فراجعها
The principle in this type [of agency] is that if the principal stipulates a condition on the agent that is beneficial in every respect, i.e., it benefits him from every angle, then the agent must observe his condition, whether the principal emphasized it with a negative or not. Its illustration: if he said 'Sell with an option' and he sold without an option, it is not permissible. And if he stipulates a condition that is entirely not beneficial, i.e., it does not benefit him at all but rather harms him, the agent is not required to observe it, whether the principal emphasized it with a negative or not. Its illustration: if he said 'Sell for a thousand on credit' or 'Do not sell it except for a thousand on credit,' and he sold it for a thousand cash, it is valid against the principal. If he stipulates a condition that is beneficial in one way and not beneficial in another, i.e., it benefits him in one way and does not benefit in another, then if he emphasized it with a negative, he must observe it; if he did not emphasize it, he need not observe it. Its illustration: if he said 'Sell in such-and-such market' and he sold it in another market, then if he did not emphasize it with a negative, i.e., he did not say 'except in such-and-such market,' and he sold in another market, it is effective against the principal; but if he emphasized it with a negative, it is not effective against the principal. End. The complete sub-branches are in it, so refer to them.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage lays down a general principle (الأصل) for conditions that a principal may place upon an agent. Conditions are categorized into three types. First, a condition that is beneficial from every aspect: the agent must adhere to it regardless of tawkīd (emphasis). Example: 'Sell with an option' – selling without option is invalid. Second, a condition that is entirely harmful or useless: the agent may disregard it even if emphasized. Example: 'Sell on credit' (which is detrimental) – selling for cash is valid. Third, a mixed condition: if the principal emphasizes it with a negative (e.g., 'except'), the agent must follow; otherwise, he may deviate. Example: 'Sell in market X' without 'except' – selling in another market is valid; with 'except' it is not. The passage notes that detailed sub-branches exist elsewhere.

Key Terms
شرط مفيد من كل وجه (sharṭ mufīd min kull wajh): A condition that benefits the principal in all aspects, such as selling with an option.
تأكيد بالنفي (ta'kīd bi-al-nafy): Emphasizing a condition by using a negative phrase like 'except' or 'do not'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A principal says 'Sell this item for a thousand on credit' without adding 'except'. If the agent sells for cash, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is invalid because the agent disobeyed.
B) The sale is valid and binding on the principal.
C) The sale is suspended pending the principal's approval.
D) The agent is liable for the difference.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule classifies principal's conditions into three types: fully beneficial (binding), fully harmful (non-binding), and mixed (binding only if emphasized).

Agent's Claim of Delivery After Principal's Death: Scope of Trustworthiness
Intra-Hanafi dispute over agent's claim after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the agent's discharge and the debtor's discharge in claims after the principal's death, and apply the refined Hanafi position.

Core Idea
An agent's claim of delivery is always accepted to clear himself, but after death it cannot clear the debtor without proof.

This passage refines a common rule about trustees. While trustees are generally believed, an agent for debt after the principal's death faces a special restriction. The earlier view completely rejected his claim, but later scholars like al-Shurnubālālī introduced a critical distinction: the agent's claim is always accepted for his own discharge, but the debtor's discharge requires evidence or heirs' confirmation. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly resolving disputes involving agents and heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule for Trustees
State the default rule: any trustee (amīn) who claims to have delivered the entrusted property to its rightful owner is believed without proof. This includes depositaries, agents, and supervisors.
This rule is based on the principle that trustees are trusted, and their statement is accepted unless proven otherwise.
2
Note the Exception in al-Ashbāh
Al-Ashbāh states that an exception exists for the agent for debt collection (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn). If he claims after the principal's death that he collected and paid the debtor during the principal's lifetime, his claim is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah).
This exception seems to treat the agent for debt differently from the agent for a specific item (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-'ayn), whose claim remains accepted.
3
Recognize al-Shurnubālālī's Refinement
Al-Shurnubālālī challenges the blanket exception. He argues that the agent's claim should be accepted for his own discharge (barā'ah) in all cases, regardless of timing. However, the effect on the debtor's discharge (barā'at al-gharīm) depends on when the claim is made.
This refinement is taken from al-Walwālijiyya and other madhhab sources.
4
Distinguish Between Agent's Discharge and Debtor's Discharge
During the principal's lifetime, the agent's claim that he collected and paid the debtor suffices to discharge both the agent and the debtor. After the principal's death, the agent's claim still discharges himself from liability, but for the debtor to be discharged, either the heirs must confirm or the agent must produce evidence.
The rationale: after death, the agent's claim affects the heirs' right to pursue the debtor, so stricter proof is needed.
5
Apply the Muftā Bihī Ruling
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is that the agent's claim of delivery is accepted for his own discharge in all cases. For the debtor's discharge, it is only accepted if the claim is made during the principal's lifetime; after death, evidence or heirs' affirmation is required.
This is the view of al-Shurnubālālī and is supported by later scholars like Abū al-Su'ūd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو زعم الوكيل قبضه وتسليمه إلى الطالب الخ قال في الأشباه كل أمين ادعى إيصال الأمانة إلى مستحقها قبل قوله كالمودع والوكيل والناظر إلا في الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا ادعى بعد موت الموكل أنه قبضه ودفعه له في حياته لم يقبل إلا ببينة بخلاف الوكيل بقبض العين والفرق في الولوالجية اهـ. وأقول : تعقبه الشرنبلالي أخذاً. من كلام الولوالجية وغيرها من كتب المذهب بأن دعوى الوكيل الإيصال تقبل لبراءته بكل حال، وأما سراية قوله على موكله ليبرأ غريمه فهو خاص بما إذا ادعى الوكيل حال حياة موكله وأما بعد موته فلا تثبت براءة الغريم إلا ببينة أو تصديق الورثة إلى آخر ما ذكره في الرسالة المسماة بمنة الجليل في قبول قول الوكيل؛ كذا في حاشية أبي السعود. قلت : وللعلامة المقدسي أيضاً رسالة في هذه المسألة ذكرها الشرنبلالي في مجموعة رسائله عقب الرسالة التي ألفها واستشهد بها على
His statement: 'If the agent claims that he collected and delivered to the claimant during his lifetime, he is not believed without proof.' [The commentator] said in al-Ashbāh: 'Every trustee who claims to have delivered the trust to its entitled person, his statement is accepted, like the depositary, the agent, and the supervisor, except in the case of the agent for collecting a debt: if he claims after the death of the principal that he collected and paid him during his lifetime, it is not accepted except with evidence, unlike the agent for collecting a specific item.' The difference is in al-Walwālijiyya. End. I say: al-Shurnubālālī criticized [this], taking from the statements of al-Walwālijiyya and other books of the madhhab, that the agent's claim of delivery is accepted for his own discharge in any case. As for the effect of his statement on his principal to discharge the debtor, it is specific to when the agent claims during the lifetime of his principal. But after his death, the discharge of the debtor is not established except by evidence or the affirmation of the heirs, up to what he mentioned in the treatise called Minnat al-Jalīl on accepting the word of the agent. Likewise in the gloss of Abū al-Su'ūd. I say: al-'Allāma al-Maqdisī also has a treatise on this issue, which al-Shurnubālālī mentioned in his collection of treatises following the treatise he authored, and he cited it as evidence.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a previous ruling that an agent for collecting debt who claims collection and delivery after the principal's death is not believed without evidence. The commentator cites al-Ashbāh which states that trustees are generally believed, but the agent for debt is an exception. However, al-Shurnubālālī refines this: the agent's claim is always accepted for his own discharge (to prove he is not liable). The only issue is that after the principal's death, his statement cannot discharge the debtor; for that, evidence or the heirs' acknowledgment is needed. During the principal's lifetime, the agent's statement can discharge both. The passage notes that al-Maqdisī also wrote on this. Thus, the relied-upon view is that the agent's claim of delivery is accepted for his own freedom from liability in all cases, but the debtor's discharge depends on timing and evidence.

Key Terms
أمين (amīn): A trustee; someone entrusted with property, like a depositary, agent, or supervisor.
وكيل بقبض العين (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-'ayn): Agent for collecting a specific physical item, as opposed to a debt.
براءة (barā'ah): Discharge from liability; freedom from obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The claim is rejected entirely without evidence.
B) The claim is accepted for the agent's own discharge but not to discharge the debtor.
C) The claim is accepted only if supported by witnesses.
D) The claim is accepted to discharge both the agent and the debtor.

Key Takeaway
The refined Hanafi position accepts the agent's claim of delivery for his own discharge in all cases, but after the principal's death, the debtor's discharge requires evidence or heirs' affirmation.

Powers and Limitations of the Agent for Debt Collection
States the powers the agent for debt collection does not have

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acts an agent for debt collection is not empowered to perform and apply the rule that his authority is limited to collection.

Core Idea
An agent for debt collection may only collect; he cannot discharge, gift, take pledges or sureties, or accept debt transfers.

This lesson presents key rulings from Hanafi fiqh regarding the scope of an agent appointed solely to collect a debt (wakil bil-qabd). Unlike an agent for sale who enjoys broader powers, the collection agent's authority is strictly limited to receiving payment. The passage enumerates specific actions he cannot undertake, and outlines how events like death of the principal or transfer of debt affect his agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of agent
Distinguish between wakil bil-qabd (agent for debt collection) and wakil bil-bay' (agent for sale). This passage applies only to the former.
2
List the prohibited acts
From the text, list five actions the agent cannot do: ibra' (discharge), hiba (gift), taking rahn (pledge), taking kafeel (surety), and accepting hawala (debt transfer).
These are void if done without authorization.
3
Note exceptions and distinctions
The agent for sale possesses all these powers. The agent for collection may collect in parts unless instructed otherwise.
4
Understand the effects of death and hawala
Agency terminates upon death of principal (claimant) but not upon death of debtor. If the principal transfers the debt via hawala, the agent loses the right to claim from both transferor and transferee.
If the debt returns to the principal due to transferee's insolvency, the agent's right revives.
5
Apply to surety and partial collection
The agent cannot claim from a surety unless the principal explicitly authorizes. Partial collection is allowed unless the principal stipulates otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يملك الوكيل بقبضه الإبراء والهبة وأخذ الرهن وملك أخذ الكفيل بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع حيث ملك الكل، وليس للوكيل بالقبض قبول الحوالة، ويصح التوكيل بالقبض والقضاء بلا رضا الخصم ولا ينعزل بموت المطلوب وينعزل بموت الطالب، فلو زعم الوكيل قبضه وتسليمه إلى الطالب حال حياته لم يصدق بلا حجة، فإن احتال الطالب بالمال بعد التوكيل على إنسان ليس للوكيل أن يطالب المحيل ،والمحتال فلو توى المال على المحال عليه وعاد الدين على المحيل فالوكيل يملك الطلب ولو كان بالمال كفيل أو أخذ الطالب كفيلاً بعد التوكيل ليس للوكيل أن يتقاضى الكفيل وللوكيل بالقبض قبض بعضه إلا إذا نص على أن لا يقبض إلا الكل معاً .
The agent for debt collection does not have the power of [granting] acquittal (ibra'), donation (hiba), taking a pledge (rahn), and taking a surety (kafeel), contrary to the agent for sale, who possesses all [these powers]. The agent for debt collection is not allowed to accept a transfer of debt (hawala). Agency for collection and payment is valid without the consent of the adversary. He is not discharged by the death of the debtor, but is discharged by the death of the claimant. If the agent claims that he collected and delivered to the claimant during the claimant's lifetime, his statement is not accepted without proof. If the claimant, after agency, transfers the debt to another person, the agent cannot demand payment from the transferor (muhil) and the transferee (muhtal). If the money is lost with the transferee (muhal 'alayh) and the debt returns to the transferor, the agent has the right to demand. If there is a surety for the money or the claimant took a surety after agency, the agent cannot claim from the surety. The agent for collection is allowed to collect part of the debt, unless it is stipulated that he must collect the entire amount together.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings (ahkam) regarding an agent appointed specifically to collect a debt (wakil bil-qabd). It begins by enumerating powers that are NOT granted to this type of agent, unlike an agent appointed to sell (wakil bil-bay'), who has broader authority. Specifically, the agent for debt collection cannot grant discharge (ibra'), make a gift (hiba) of the debt, accept a pledge (rahn) as security, or take a surety (kafeel). Additionally, he cannot accept a transfer of debt (hawala). The passage then addresses the validity of such agency: it is valid even without the consent of the person from whom the debt is to be collected. The agent is not discharged by the death of the debtor, but is discharged by the death of the claimant (the principal). If the agent claims to have collected and delivered the debt during the claimant's life, his word is not accepted without evidence (beyond his own oath), because he is acting as an agent for the principal, and his claim affects the liability of the debtor. The text then discusses cases involving a transfer of debt (hawala): if the claimant transfers the debt to a third party after appointing the agent, the agent loses the right to claim from the transferor or the transferee. However, if the debt returns to the transferor due to the insolvency of the transferee, the agent's right to demand revives. Similarly, if there is a surety for the debt, the agent cannot demand payment from the surety. Finally, the agent may collect the debt in installments unless specifically instructed to collect the entire amount at once.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالقبض (al-wakīl bil-qabḍ): An agent appointed specifically to collect a debt owed to the principal.
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): The act of releasing the debtor from liability by the creditor or his agent.
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt from one person to another, whereby the agent loses authority to collect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The agent can still claim from the transferor.
B) The agent can claim from the transferee.
C) The agent cannot claim from either the transferor or the transferee.
D) The agent's agency terminates automatically.

Key Takeaway
An agent for debt collection is authorized only to collect; any additional act like discharge, gift, or acceptance of security requires explicit permission.

Distinctions Between Agent for Debt Collection and Agent for Sale
Listing specific rulings differentiating two types of agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state three specific legal differences between an agent for debt collection and an agent for sale in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An agent's specific role determines liability, testimonial capacity, and the validity of suretyship.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq highlights key practical differences between an agent appointed solely to collect a debt and an agent who sells goods and collects the price. Although both handle monetary transactions, their legal capacities diverge in matters of suretyship, testimony, and liability for returned goods. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying agency rules in commercial and judicial settings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agents
Note the two agents being compared: the agent for debt collection (wakīl bi-qabḍ al-dayn) and the agent for sale who also collects the price (wakīl bi'l-bay‘ wa qabḍ al-thaman).
2
Analyze the first difference: suretyship
Read the ruling: If the agent for collection stands surety for the buyer, it is valid; if the agent for sale does so, it is invalid. Consider why the agent for sale cannot guarantee the buyer's payment without compromising the sale.
Reference: al-Khāniyya
3
Analyze the second difference: testimony
The agent for collection's testimony against the debtor regarding the debt is accepted, but the agent for sale's testimony is not. Reflect on the conflict of interest that disqualifies the selling agent.
Reference: al-Bazzāziyya
4
Analyze the third difference: liability upon return of goods
If the agent sells and collects, then the goods are returned due to a defect after paying the principal, the buyer can claim the price from the agent. The agent for collection faces no such claim. Understand that the selling agent is the immediate recipient and thus liable.
Reference: al-Qunyah
5
Synthesize the principle
Summarize that the agent for debt collection is a mere collector with limited authority, while the agent for sale has broader authority and thus greater legal responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن الوكيل بقبض الدين يخالف الوكيل بالبيع وقبض الثمن في مسائل فلو كفل الوكيل بقبض الثمن المشتري صحت، ولو كفل الوكيل بالبيع لم تصح كما في الخانية. وتقبل شهادة الوكيل بقبض الدين به على المديون في شهادات البزازية بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع ولو باع الوكيل وقبض الثمن ثم رد المبيع بعيب بعد ما دفع الثمن للموكل فللمشتري مطالبة الوكيل بخلاف الوكيل بقبض الثمن لا مطالبة عليه كما في القنية.
The summary is that the agent for debt collection differs from the agent for sale and collection of price in several matters: If the agent for collection of the price stands surety (kafalah) for the buyer, it is valid; but if the agent for sale stands surety for the buyer, it is not valid, as in al-Khaniyya. The testimony of the agent for debt collection is accepted against the debtor regarding the debt in the testimonies of al-Bazzaziyya, contrary to the agent for sale. If the agent sold and collected the price, then the goods were returned due to a defect after he had paid the price to the principal, the buyer can demand [the price] from the agent; contrary to the agent for collection of the price, against whom there is no claim, as in al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the differences between the agent for debt collection (wakil bil-qabd) and the agent for sale (wakil bil-bay') who also collects the price. It enumerates specific scenarios where their legal positions diverge. First, regarding suretyship (kafalah): if the agent who collects the price (but is not the seller) guarantees the buyer's payment, it is valid because he is merely a collector. However, if the agent who is also the seller guarantees the buyer's payment, it is not valid because the contract of sale would be compromised by the agent's personal guarantee. Second, the testimony of the agent for debt collection is accepted against the debtor regarding the debt, as recorded in al-Bazzaziyya, unlike the agent for sale. This is because the agent for debt collection is an attesting witness to the debt, while the agent for sale may have a conflict of interest. Third, if the agent sells and collects the price, then the goods are returned due to a defect after he has handed over the price to the principal, the buyer can reclaim the price from the agent (since the agent is the immediate recipient). In contrast, an agent who only collects the price (not the seller) is not liable for the return of the price after a defect is discovered.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): Suretyship; the agent's guaranteeing the buyer's payment.
شهادة الوكيل بقبض الدين (shahādatu l-wakīli bi-qabḍi d-dayn): The testimony of the agent for debt collection regarding the debt, which is accepted against the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the agent for sale (who also collects the price) stands surety for the buyer, what is the ruling?
A) It is valid because the agent is the seller.
B) It is not valid.
C) It is valid only if the principal consents.
D) It is valid but the agent must refund the price to the seller.

Key Takeaway
The agent for debt collection and the agent for sale differ in three key rulings: validity of suretyship, acceptance of testimony, and liability for returned goods.

The Three Categories of Principal's Conditions
Explains classification of conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditions imposed by a principal into three types based on benefit and emphasis, and derive the ruling for each.

Core Idea
A condition's benefit to the principal determines whether the agent must obey it; emphasis via negation tips the balance for mixed-benefit conditions.

In agency (wakalah), a principal often imposes conditions to protect his interest. However, not all conditions are equally binding on the agent. The Hanafi school classifies conditions based on their benefit to the principal, and the presence or absence of a negation (akkadahu bi-n-nafy) matters for partially beneficial conditions. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out the principle (asl) with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of conditions
The passage divides conditions into: wholly beneficial (e.g., selling with khiyar), wholly harmful (e.g., selling on credit when spot is better), and mixed (e.g., selling in a specific market).
2
Determine the effect of emphasis (ta'kid bi-n-nafy)
Emphasis via negation (e.g., 'لا' or 'إلا') only matters for mixed-benefit conditions. It has no effect on wholly beneficial or wholly harmful conditions.
The term 'akkadahu bi-n-nafy' means emphasizing with a negative particle indicating exclusivity.
3
Apply the rule to the given examples
For each example in the text, identify which category it belongs to and whether the agent's action is valid (sahih) or invalid (ghayr sahih) against the principal.
Examples: selling without khiyar, selling spot when told credit, selling in a different market.
4
Derive the general principle
The agent must observe conditions that are wholly beneficial. He may ignore wholly harmful conditions. For mixed conditions, he must obey if emphasized; otherwise he may act in the principal's best interest.
This principle is based on the Hanafi understanding of the agent's duty to act in the principal's benefit.
5
Practice with a new scenario
Consider: Principal says 'Sell my car for $10,000 cash only.' Agent sells for $12,000 deferred. Categorize and rule.
This is a mixed condition (cash vs. higher price) with emphasis ('only'), so the agent must obey, making the deferred sale invalid against the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذا النوع إن الموكل إذا شرط على الوكيل شرطاً مفيداً من كل وجه بأن كان ينفعه من كل وجه فإنه يجب على الوكيل مراعاة شرطه، أكده بالنفي أو لم يؤكده. بيانه فيما إذا قال بعه بخيار فباعه بغير خيار لا يجوز، وإن شرط في العقد شرطاً لا يفيد أصلاً بأن كان لا ينفعه بوجه بل يضره لا يجب على الوكيل مراعاته، أكده الموكل بالنفي أو لم يؤكده . بيانه فيما إذا قال بعه بألف نسيئة أو قال لا تبعه إلا بألف نسيئة فباعه بألف نقداً يجوز على الأمر، فإذا شرط شرطاً يفيد من وجه ولا يفيد من وجه بأن كان ينفع من وجه ولا ينفع من وجه، إن أكده بالنفي يجب مراعاته، وإن لم يؤكده لا يجب مراعاته بيانه فيما إذا قال بعه في سوق كذا فباعه في سوق آخر، فإن لم يؤكده بالنفي بأن لم يقل إلا في سوق كذا فباعه في سوق آخر ينفذ على الأمر، وإن أكده بالنفي لا ينفذ على الآمر اهـ.
The principle in this type [of conditions] is: If the principal imposes on the agent a condition that is beneficial from every aspect, i.e., it benefits him in every way, then the agent must observe his condition, whether the principal emphasized it with negation or not. Its illustration: If he said, 'Sell it with a stipulation of option (khiyar),' and he sold it without option, it is not permissible. If he imposes a condition that is not beneficial at all, i.e., it does not benefit him in any way but rather harms him, the agent is not required to observe it, whether the principal emphasized it with negation or not. Its illustration: If he said, 'Sell it for a thousand deferred payment (nasi'ah),' or said, 'Do not sell it except for a thousand deferred payment,' and he sold it for a thousand spot payment (naqdan), it is valid as against the principal. If he imposes a condition that is beneficial in one way but not in another, i.e., it benefits in one way but not in another, then if he emphasized it with negation, it must be observed; if he did not emphasize it, it need not be observed. Its illustration: If he said, 'Sell it in such-and-such a market,' and he sold it in another market, then if he did not emphasize it with negation (i.e., did not say 'only in such-and-such a market'), his sale in another market is valid as against the principal; if he emphasized it with negation, it is not valid as against the principal. (End).
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle (asl) regarding the effect of conditions imposed by the principal (muwakkil) on the agent (wakil). The rulings are divided into three categories based on the condition's benefit to the principal. First, a condition that is beneficial from every aspect (like selling with a stipulation of option) must be observed by the agent; if the agent violates it, the contract is not valid as against the principal. The presence or absence of a negation (like 'only') does not matter. Second, a condition that is not beneficial at all but rather harmful (like selling on deferred payment when spot is better) does not bind the agent; if the agent sells in a better way (spot), the sale is valid and binding on the principal. Again, negation does not affect this ruling because the condition is inherently harmful. Third, a condition that is partially beneficial and partially not (like selling in a specific market) requires analysis: if the principal emphasized the condition using a negation (e.g., 'do not sell except in this market'), the agent must obey; if he did not use a negation, the agent may act in the principal's best interest. The text provides clear examples for each case.

Key Terms
أكده بالنفي (akkadahu bi-n-nafy): Emphasized the condition through a negative particle (e.g., 'لا' or 'إلا') indicating exclusivity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the principal says 'Sell it for a thousand deferred payment' (nasi'ah) but the agent sells it for a thousand spot (naqdan), what is the ruling?
A) The sale is invalid because the agent violated the condition.
B) The sale is valid and binding on the principal because the condition was not beneficial at all.
C) The sale is valid only if the principal agreed afterward.
D) The sale is valid but the agent is liable for the difference.

Key Takeaway
The agent's duty to obey a condition depends on the condition's benefit: wholly beneficial must be obeyed; wholly harmful can be ignored; mixed requires obedience only if emphasized with a negation.

Agent's Claim After Principal's Death: Agent Discharged, Debtor Not
Refinement of ruling on agent's claim after principal's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the refined Hanafi ruling on an agent's claim after the principal's death and explain why the debtor is not automatically discharged.

Core Idea
The agent's word is taken for his own discharge, but the debtor remains liable unless proven — the two are separate.

This passage resolves a critical nuance in Hanafi law: while trustees (umana) are generally believed when claiming delivery of trust property, the effect of such a claim differs for the agent's own liability versus the debtor's liability. After the principal's death, the agent is discharged from liability if he claims collection and delivery, but the debtor remains liable unless he brings proof (bayyinah) or the heirs confirm. This refinement by al-Shurunbulali clarifies an apparent contradiction in earlier texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general trustee rule from al-Ashbah
Note that al-Ashbah states every trustee (ameen) is believed when claiming delivery of the trust to its rightful owner. This includes depositary (mudi'), agent (wakil), and supervisor (nazir). The exception is the agent for debt collection after the principal's death.
The general rule is based on the principle of trust (amanah).
2
Note the exception for debt collection after death
Al-Ashbah states that if the agent for collecting a debt claims after the principal's death that he collected and paid during the principal's life, his word is not accepted without proof (bayyinah). This is contrasted with the agent for a physical object, who is believed.
This exception initially seems to conflict with the general trustee rule.
3
Understand the refinement: separation of agent's discharge from debtor's discharge
Al-Shurunbulali resolves the conflict: the agent's claim is always accepted for his own discharge (bara'ah) from liability. However, the effect (sirāyah) of his statement on the principal to discharge the debtor is limited: during the principal's life, the agent's word can discharge the debtor; after death, it cannot without proof or confirmation from the heirs.
Thus the agent is believed for his own liability, but the debtor's liability is separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو زعم الوكيل قبضه وتسليمه إلى الطالب الخ قال في الأشباه كل أمين ادعى إيصال الأمانة إلى مستحقها قبل قوله كالمودع والوكيل والناظر إلا في الوكيل بقبض الدين إذا ادعى بعد موت الموكل أنه قبضه ودفعه له في حياته لم يقبل إلا ببينة بخلاف الوكيل بقبض العين والفرق في الولوالجية اهـ. وأقول : تعقبه الشرنبلالي أخذاً. من كلام الولوالجية وغيرها من كتب المذهب بأن دعوى الوكيل الإيصال تقبل لبراءته بكل حال، وأما سراية قوله على موكله ليبرأ غريمه فهو خاص بما إذا ادعى الوكيل حال حياة موكله وأما بعد موته فلا تثبت براءة الغريم إلا ببينة أو تصديق الورثة إلى آخر ما ذكره في الرسالة المسماة بمنة الجليل في قبول قول الوكيل؛ كذا في حاشية أبي السعود. قلت : وللعلامة المقدسي أيضاً رسالة في هذه المسألة ذكرها الشرنبلالي في مجموعة رسائله عقب الرسالة التي ألفها واستشهد بها على
His statement: 'If the agent claims that he collected and delivered to the claimant, etc.' He said in al-Ashbah: Every trustee (ameen) who claims to have delivered the trust to its rightful owner, his statement is accepted, like the depositary (mudi'), agent (wakil), and supervisor (nazir), except in the case of the agent for debt collection. If he claims after the death of the principal that he collected and paid him during his lifetime, it is not accepted without proof, contrary to the agent for collecting a physical object; and the difference is in al-Walwaljiyyah. (End.) I say: Al-Shurunbulali criticized this, taking from the words of al-Walwaljiyyah and other books of the school, that the agent's claim of delivery is accepted for his own discharge in any case. As for the effect of his statement on his principal to discharge the debtor, it is specific to when the agent claims during the lifetime of his principal. After his death, the discharge of the debtor is not established except by proof (bayyinah) or the acknowledgment of the heirs, as mentioned in the treatise titled 'Mann al-Jaleel' on accepting the word of the agent; thus in the gloss of Abu al-Su'ud. I say: The scholar al-Maqdisi also wrote a treatise on this issue, which al-Shurunbulali mentioned in his collection of treatises after the treatise he authored and used as evidence for what he claimed.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the earlier ruling that if the agent claims collection and delivery during the principal's lifetime, his statement is not accepted without proof. It refers to the principle in al-Ashbah that trustees (umana) are generally believed when they claim delivery, except for the agent for debt collection after the principal's death. The text then presents a critical refinement (ta'aqub) by al-Shurunbulali, who argues that the agent's claim should be accepted for his own discharge (bara'ah) in all cases. However, the effect of the agent's statement on the debtor's liability differs: during the principal's life, the agent's claim can serve to discharge the debtor (since the principal can confirm or deny), but after the principal's death, the debtor is only discharged if there is evidence (bayyinah) or if the heirs confirm. This position is supported by the treatise 'Mann al-Jaleel' and the gloss of Abu al-Su'ud. The passage also mentions that al-Maqdisi wrote a separate treatise on this matter, which is found in Shurunbulali's collection. This is a good example of intra-madhab scholarly refinement, where later Hanafi jurists fine-tune earlier fatwas based on nuanced understanding of the sources.

Key Terms
الأمين (al-amīn): A trustee: one entrusted with property, like a depositary or agent.
السراية (as-sirāyah): The effect of the agent's statement extending to the principal or debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refined Hanafi position, if the agent for debt collection claims after the principal's death that he collected and delivered the debt during the principal's life, what happens?
A) The agent is not believed at all.
B) The agent is believed for his own discharge, but the debtor is not discharged without proof.
C) Both the agent and the debtor are discharged automatically.
D) The debtor is discharged if the agent is truthful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, after the principal's death, the agent for debt collection is believed for his own discharge from liability, but the debtor is not discharged unless he brings proof (bayyinah) or the heirs confirm.

How Attribution Determines Liability in Agency Contracts
Rulings on attribution, contravention, and liability in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on attribution of contracts in agency and apply it to determine who bears liability (al-uhdah) in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Liability follows attribution: if the agent attributes the contract to himself, he bears liability; if to the principal, the principal bears it.

In Hanafi law, an agent (wakil) acts on behalf of a principal. A key question is: who is liable for obligations arising from the contract? This lesson examines rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on how the agent's statement determines liability, including special cases like contravention and specific item purchases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal context
This passage from Kitab al-Wakalah presents several rulings. First, note the oath scenario that reveals the agent may bear personal liability.
The oath shows that the agent can be personally liable for debts if he attributed the contract to himself.
2
Extract the rule on attribution
The text states that the agent must add (attribute) the contract to the principal for it to be binding on the principal. If he adds to himself, the liability attaches to the agent.
This is the core principle: al-idafa determines liability.
3
Analyze the contravention case
An agent instructed not to accept liability does so anyway. The text says it does not bind the principal; Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar says the agent becomes fuduli and the contract is contingent on ratification.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the relied-upon view is contingency.
4
Examine the condition on rights
A stipulation that no rights attach to the agent is invalid. This shows that liability (al-uhdah) is inherent to the agency contract and cannot be contractually removed from the agent.
This reinforces the importance of attribution.
5
Apply the exception for specific purchase
If the agent is appointed to buy a specific item, the contract and ownership go to the principal even without explicit attribution. This is an exception that protects the principal.
This is based on al-Muhit and prevents injustice when the item is clearly for the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
حتى لو حلف المشتري ما للموكل عليه شيء كان باراً في يمينه ولو حلف ما للوكيل عليه شيء كان حانثاً ا هـ . والمراد بقوله فيما يضيفه الوكيل في كل عقد لا بد من إضافته إليه لينفذ على الموكل، وليس المراد ظاهر العبارة من أنه قد يضيفه وقد لا يضيفه فإن أضافه إلى نفسه تتعلق بالوكيل وإن أضافه إلى موكله تتعلق بالموكل كما فهمه ابن الملك في شرح المجمع لما في الخلاصة والبزازية : وكيل شراء العبد جاء إلى مالكه فقال بعت هذا العبد من الموكل وقال الوكيل قبلت لا يلزم الموكل لأنه خالف حيث أمره أن لا ترجع إليه العهدة وقد رجع قال أبو القاسم الصفار والصحيح أن الوكيل يصير فضولياً ويتوقف العقد على إجازة الموكل ا هـ . وفي الجوهرة وكله بالبيع والشراء على أن لا تتعلق به الحقوق لا يصح هذا الشرط. وقيد بالوكيل لأن الرسول لا ترجع الحقوق إليه ولو ادعى أنه رسول وقال البائع إنه وكيل وطالبه بالثمن فالقول للمشتري والبينة على البائع ؛ إليه أشير في بيوع الخانية. وشرطه الإضافة إلى مرسله لما في البزازية والرسول في البيع والطلاق والعتاق والنكاح إذا أخرج الكلام مخرج الوكالة بأن أضاف إلى نفسه بأن قال طلقتك وبعتك وزوجت فلانة منك لا يجوز لأن الرسالة لا تتضمن الوكالة لأنها فوقها وإن أخرج مخرج الرسالة جاز بأن يقول إن مرسلي يقول بعت وفي المحيط : الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه يقع العقد والملك للموكل وإن لم يضف
Book of Agency. Even if the buyer swore that the principal does not have anything against him, he would be truthful in his oath. And if he swore that the agent does not have anything against him, he would be sinful. The intended meaning of his statement is that whatever the agent adds in every contract must be added to him for it to be binding on the principal. And the apparent meaning of the expression is not that he may add it or may not add it, because if he adds it to himself it pertains to the agent, and if he adds it to his principal it pertains to the principal, as understood by Ibn al-Malik in Sharh al-Majma'. Due to what is in al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah: An agent for the purchase of a slave came to its owner and said, 'I have sold this slave from the principal,' and the agent said, 'I accept,' it does not bind the principal because he contravened his instruction that he not return to him the liability (al-uhdah) and he returned. Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar said: The correct view is that the agent becomes an unauthorized agent (fuduli) and the contract is contingent upon the ratification of the principal. And in al-Jawharah: If he appoints him as an agent for sale and purchase on the condition that rights not attach to him, this condition is invalid. And it is restricted to the agent because the messenger (rasul) does not have rights return to him. And if he claimed that he is a messenger and the seller said he is an agent and demanded the price from him, the statement is of the buyer and the burden of proof is on the seller; it is alluded to in Buyu' al-Khaniyyah. And its condition is attribution to his sender, due to what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: The messenger in sale, divorce, manumission, and marriage, if he puts the speech in the form of agency by attributing it to himself, saying, 'I divorce you, I sell you, I marry so-and-so to you,' it is not permissible because messengership does not entail agency since it is above it. And if he puts it in the form of messengership, it is permissible, such as saying, 'My sender says, I sell.' And in al-Muhit: The agent for the purchase of a specific item, the contract and ownership occur for the principal even if he does not attribute.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Wakalah in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several basic rulings on agency. First, it discusses an oath scenario: if the buyer swears that the principal owes him nothing, he is truthful; if he swears the agent owes him nothing, he is sinful because the agent may be liable. Then it clarifies that the agent must attribute the contract to the principal for it to be binding. The phrase 'what the agent adds' refers to the contract; he must say 'I sold for my principal' or similar. If he attributes to himself, he becomes personally liable. The passage then quotes al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah with a case: an agent for purchase told the seller 'you sold to the principal' and the agent said 'I accept'. This contravenes his mandate if he was told not to accept liability. Therefore, it does not bind the principal. Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar holds it becomes contingent on ratification. Next, al-Jawharah states that stipulating that no rights attach to the agent is invalid. Then it distinguishes the agent from the messenger: the messenger acts purely as a conduit and rights do not attach to him. A dispute between seller and buyer about whether the intermediary is agent or messenger is settled by taking the buyer's statement with seller's burden of proof. The messenger must attribute to his sender; if he uses agent-style language, it is invalid. Finally, al-Muhit confirms that if an agent is appointed to buy a specific item, the contract and ownership are for the principal even without explicit attribution. All these rulings follow the Hanafi school. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as they are standard rulings in the core books.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): A person appointed by another to act on his behalf in a transaction, with the legal effects of the act pertaining to the principal if attributed correctly.
العهدة (al-uhdah): Liability or responsibility for a claim or debt arising from a contract, such as the obligation to deliver the purchased item or pay the price.
الإضافة (al-idafa): Attribution of the contract to oneself or to the principal; the agent must add the contract to the principal for it to be binding on him.
فضولي (fuduli): A person who acts without authorization; the contract is contingent on ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an agent is appointed to buy a specific item (شيء بعينه) and he does not attribute the contract to the principal, the contract and ownership are for whom?
A) The agent
B) The principal
C) The agent and principal jointly
D) The seller

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, the agent's liability follows attribution; attribution to the principal transfers liability to the principal, while contravention or self-attribution makes the agent liable, though the principal may ratify.

6 min
Agent vs Messenger: Language and Liability
Distinction between agent and messenger in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an agent (wakil) and a messenger (rasul) in Hanafi law and identify the correct linguistic form for each.

Core Idea
A messenger is above an agent: he merely conveys the principal's speech and bears no liability, but he must use messenger-style language.

Hanafi law distinguishes between an agent (wakil) who has authority to act independently and a messenger (rasul) who is a pure conduit. This distinction affects liability and the required language for contracts. This lesson explores the rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on how to identify each role and the consequences of using the wrong form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
The text states that a messenger (rasul) is above an agent (wakil) in that rights do not revert to him; he is a pure intermediary. An agent can have rights and liabilities attached.
This is a key conceptual difference.
2
Examine the dispute about status
If a person claims to be a messenger but the seller says he is an agent, whose statement is taken? The text says the buyer's (the alleged messenger's) statement is taken with the burden of proof on the seller.
This rule protects the person who claims to be a messenger from liability.
3
Analyze the language requirement for a messenger
A messenger must use messenger-style language: 'My sender says: I sell.' If he uses agent-style language ('I sell'), it is invalid because messengership does not entail agency.
The form (sighah) determines the legal effect.
4
Apply the condition of attribution for messenger
The passage states that a condition of the messenger is attribution to his sender (al-idafa ila mursilihi). This is similar to the agent's requirement but with different consequences.
Attribution shifts liability to the principal.
5
Synthesize the two roles
Knowing whether an intermediary is an agent or messenger affects who is liable. The burden of proof and language are the key indicators.
These rulings are from al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Muhit.

Source Text

كتاب الوكالة
حتى لو حلف المشتري ما للموكل عليه شيء كان باراً في يمينه ولو حلف ما للوكيل عليه شيء كان حانثاً ا هـ . والمراد بقوله فيما يضيفه الوكيل في كل عقد لا بد من إضافته إليه لينفذ على الموكل، وليس المراد ظاهر العبارة من أنه قد يضيفه وقد لا يضيفه فإن أضافه إلى نفسه تتعلق بالوكيل وإن أضافه إلى موكله تتعلق بالموكل كما فهمه ابن الملك في شرح المجمع لما في الخلاصة والبزازية : وكيل شراء العبد جاء إلى مالكه فقال بعت هذا العبد من الموكل وقال الوكيل قبلت لا يلزم الموكل لأنه خالف حيث أمره أن لا ترجع إليه العهدة وقد رجع قال أبو القاسم الصفار والصحيح أن الوكيل يصير فضولياً ويتوقف العقد على إجازة الموكل ا هـ . وفي الجوهرة وكله بالبيع والشراء على أن لا تتعلق به الحقوق لا يصح هذا الشرط. وقيد بالوكيل لأن الرسول لا ترجع الحقوق إليه ولو ادعى أنه رسول وقال البائع إنه وكيل وطالبه بالثمن فالقول للمشتري والبينة على البائع ؛ إليه أشير في بيوع الخانية. وشرطه الإضافة إلى مرسله لما في البزازية والرسول في البيع والطلاق والعتاق والنكاح إذا أخرج الكلام مخرج الوكالة بأن أضاف إلى نفسه بأن قال طلقتك وبعتك وزوجت فلانة منك لا يجوز لأن الرسالة لا تتضمن الوكالة لأنها فوقها وإن أخرج مخرج الرسالة جاز بأن يقول إن مرسلي يقول بعت وفي المحيط : الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه يقع العقد والملك للموكل وإن لم يضف
Book of Agency. Even if the buyer swore that the principal does not have anything against him, he would be truthful in his oath. And if he swore that the agent does not have anything against him, he would be sinful. The intended meaning of his statement is that whatever the agent adds in every contract must be added to him for it to be binding on the principal. And the apparent meaning of the expression is not that he may add it or may not add it, because if he adds it to himself it pertains to the agent, and if he adds it to his principal it pertains to the principal, as understood by Ibn al-Malik in Sharh al-Majma'. Due to what is in al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah: An agent for the purchase of a slave came to its owner and said, 'I have sold this slave from the principal,' and the agent said, 'I accept,' it does not bind the principal because he contravened his instruction that he not return to him the liability (al-uhdah) and he returned. Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar said: The correct view is that the agent becomes an unauthorized agent (fuduli) and the contract is contingent upon the ratification of the principal. And in al-Jawharah: If he appoints him as an agent for sale and purchase on the condition that rights not attach to him, this condition is invalid. And it is restricted to the agent because the messenger (rasul) does not have rights return to him. And if he claimed that he is a messenger and the seller said he is an agent and demanded the price from him, the statement is of the buyer and the burden of proof is on the seller; it is alluded to in Buyu' al-Khaniyyah. And its condition is attribution to his sender, due to what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: The messenger in sale, divorce, manumission, and marriage, if he puts the speech in the form of agency by attributing it to himself, saying, 'I divorce you, I sell you, I marry so-and-so to you,' it is not permissible because messengership does not entail agency since it is above it. And if he puts it in the form of messengership, it is permissible, such as saying, 'My sender says, I sell.' And in al-Muhit: The agent for the purchase of a specific item, the contract and ownership occur for the principal even if he does not attribute.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Wakalah in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several basic rulings on agency. First, it discusses an oath scenario: if the buyer swears that the principal owes him nothing, he is truthful; if he swears the agent owes him nothing, he is sinful because the agent may be liable. Then it clarifies that the agent must attribute the contract to the principal for it to be binding. The phrase 'what the agent adds' refers to the contract; he must say 'I sold for my principal' or similar. If he attributes to himself, he becomes personally liable. The passage then quotes al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah with a case: an agent for purchase told the seller 'you sold to the principal' and the agent said 'I accept'. This contravenes his mandate if he was told not to accept liability. Therefore, it does not bind the principal. Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar holds it becomes contingent on ratification. Next, al-Jawharah states that stipulating that no rights attach to the agent is invalid. Then it distinguishes the agent from the messenger: the messenger acts purely as a conduit and rights do not attach to him. A dispute between seller and buyer about whether the intermediary is agent or messenger is settled by taking the buyer's statement with seller's burden of proof. The messenger must attribute to his sender; if he uses agent-style language, it is invalid. Finally, al-Muhit confirms that if an agent is appointed to buy a specific item, the contract and ownership are for the principal even without explicit attribution. All these rulings follow the Hanafi school. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as they are standard rulings in the core books.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): A person appointed by another to act on his behalf with independent authority; rights may attach to him.
رسول (rasul): A pure intermediary who merely conveys the speech of the principal; no rights attach to him.
الإضافة (al-idafa): Attribution of the speech to the sender (mursil). For a messenger, it is a condition to say 'my sender says'.
الرسالة (al-risalah): The act of conveying a message; it does not include agency (wakalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what language must a messenger use in a sale contract?
A) 'I sell this to you.'
B) 'My sender says: I sell.'
C) 'I am selling for my principal.'
D) 'Take this from me.'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a messenger is a pure conduit who bears no liability and must use messenger-style language; using agent-style language invalidates the action.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Agent's Attribution and Liability
Intra-Hanafi dispute on attribution binding and liability rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi on whether an agent's attribution of a contract to the principal binds immediately, and explain the relied-upon rule on liability when the principal is present.

Core Idea
Attribution to principal by an authorized agent binds immediately; liability follows the receiver of the price.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does an agent's act of adding the contract to the principal (rather than himself) bind the principal without ratification? The editor favors immediate binding, citing al-Zayla'i. The passage also clarifies that, contrary to some opinions, the presence of the principal does not automatically shift liability; rather, liability rests on whoever takes the price. We will unpack both the dispute and the settled ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine what exactly is contested: whether the agent's attribution of the contract to the principal makes the contract binding on the principal immediately or requires ratification from the principal.
2
Trace the proofs for each position
The opposing view (Qadi Khan, al-Khulasah) holds that the agent contravened instructions, becoming a fuduli, so ratification is needed. The Hanafi response (al-Zayla'i, editor) argues the instruction only concerned liability, not attribution, and an authorized agent's acts bind the principal directly.
Note the analogy with the fuduli and the distinction between authorization and lack thereof.
3
Evaluate the tarjih
The editor chooses the immediate-binding view as the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). This is based on the principle that authorization makes the agent's actions directly attributable to the principal.
4
Apply the correct ruling on liability when principal present
From al-Jawharah: liability (al-uhdah) is on the one who receives the price, not on the one who conducts the contract. This overrides views that the principal is liable if present.
Practical consequence: the agent may be liable even if the principal is present, if the agent took the price.

ثم مؤيد للتوفيق والله تعالى أعلم. وفي حاشية أبي السعود وتعبير ابن الكمال بقوله «يكتفي بالإضافة إلى نفسه صريح في أن إضافته إلى نفسه ليس بلازم فيتجه ما ذكره ابن الملك، ويسقط ما اعترضه في البحر، وما في الخلاصة والبزازية لا ينافي جواز الإضافة إلى كل منهما وإن كان اللزوم على الموكل فيما إذا لم يضف الوكيل العقد إلى نفسه بأن أضافه إلى الموكل يتوقف على صدور الإجازة منه. رأيت في الزيلعي من باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء التصريح بعدم اللزوم حيث قال في شرح قول المصنف «ولو وكله بشراء شيء بعينه لا يشتريه لنفسه ما نصه : بخلاف ما لو وكله أن . يزوجه امرأة معينة حيث جاز له أن يتزوج بها لأن النكاح الذي أتى به الوكيل غير داخل تحت أمره لأن الداخل تحت الوكالة نكاح مضاف إلى الموكل وفي الوكالة بالشراء الداخل فيها شراء مطلق غير مقيد بالإضافة إلى أحد، فكل شيء أتى به لا يكون مخالفاً الخ. فهو صريح فيما ذكره ابن ملك، أيضاً في أن الوكيل إذا أضاف العقد إلى الموكل لا يكون مخالفاً ويلزمه العقد ولا يتوقف على إجازته خلافاً لما سبق على الخلاصة والبزازية .اهـ. ملخصاً. أقول وفي نور العين رامزاً للجامع الأصغر : أمره بشراء من بألف فقال مالكه بعت قني هذا من فلان الموكل فقال الوكيل قبلت لزم الوكيل إذا أمره الوكيل أن يقبل عن نفسه ليلزم العهدة على الوكيل فخالف بقبوله على موكله. قاضيخان : فيه نظر وينبغي أن يلزم الموكل أو يتوقف على إجازته إذ الوكيل لما خالف صار كأن البائع قال ابتداء بعت عبدي من فلان بكذا، وقال الوكيل قبلت يتوقف على إجازة الموكل ولا يصير الوكيل مشترياً لنفسه يقول الحقير أصاب في إيراد النظر لكنه أهمل جانب قوله يلزم الموكل حيث لم يعلله بل أفاد بما ذكره
